ERRATA IN " BRITISH RAINFALL," 1937.
p. 134. The entry in Table XXVII for July, 1937, should read 2.82.
p.205. N. Lancashire, 2. Hawkshead (Foundation Sch.) for 57.97 read 59.17.
p. 222. Cardigan, 2. Ponterwyd (The Mill) for 74 read 740.
p. 258. Co. Louth, 2. Dundalk (Ravensdale) should read Dundalk (Annaskea Res.).

BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938
PART I
THE WORK
OF THE
BRITISH RAINFALL
ORGANIZATION
BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
PART I.
THE WORK OF THE BRITISH RAINFALL ORGANIZATION.
GEORGE JAMES SYMONS, F.R.S., founder of the British Rainfall
Organization and Editor of British Rainfall from 1860 to 1900, was
born on August 6th, 1838. The centenary of his birth was commemorated in the Quarterly Journal of the Royal Meteorological
Society for October, 1938, and in the Meteorological Magazine for
August, 1938. In the Quarterly Journal Mr. E. G. Bilham gave an
account of the life and work of G. J. Symons ; Dr. C. E. P. Brooks and
Miss T. M. Hunt contributed an -article on " G. J. Symons as a
Bibliographer " and Mr. E. L. Hawke gave an account of the " Gift
to the Society by Mr. H. Sowerby Wallis of books and papers formerly
the property of Mr. Symons." In the Meteorological Magazine Dr.
H. R. Mill, the distinguished successor of Symons as Director of the
British Rainfall Organization, wrote on " The Scientific Work of
George James Symons, F.R.S." The portrait which forms the frontispiece to this volume is reproduced by courtesy of Mr. H. Sowerby
Wallis from a photograph in his possession.
RAINFALL IN EIRE. With the establishment of a Meteorological Service in Eire in April, 1937, the rainfall stations in that
country came under the control of the Director of the Meteorological
Service, Mr. A. H. Nagle. Rainfall records for Eire are therefore
included in this volume by arrangement with Mr. Nagle.
INDEX OF RIVER BASINS IN GENERAL TABLE. In
the General Table (Part III) stations are arranged by counties, and
the stations within a given county are arranged by river divisions.
This mode of arrangement dates from the early days of British Rainfall
and has been found, on the whole, to work well. Unfortunately,
however, county boundaries do not in general coincide with the
boundaries of river basins ; consequently it is usually necessary to
consult the records for more than one county if it is desired to assemble
all the readings available for a given basin. In order to facilitate
work of this kind an index of river basins, was included in British
RAINFALL RECORDS, 1938 XI
Rainfall, 1937, and is reproduced after the explanatory note to the
General Table, Part III. The index shows the counties of which some
part is included in each river basin, and it should be used in conjunction
with the alphabetical index of counties i'mmediately following.
INDEX MAPS OF STATIONS. An important part of the work
of handling the rainfall returns is that of denning the positions of the
various stations. During the year a new set of index maps on a scale
of two miles to one inch has been completed. The position of each
station is obtained from information given by the observer on Section C
of Form 1090. In some cases, especially where the gauge is situated
on the open moors, this information has not proved to be sufficiently
precise, and a tracing from the Ordnance map has then been forwarded
to the observer, so that the position could be marked thereon. When
a station is inspected the position is marked on a folding map on a
scale of one inch to the mile and the positions transferred later to the
smaller scale maps. The new series of index maps has been used in
the preparation of index maps on the same scale as the monthly
plotting maps. The new series has already proved of special service
in defining to Catchment Boards and others the localities where
additional records are likely to be of most service.
CHANGES IN COUNTY BOUNDARIES. As a result of the
1929 Local Government Act, the boundaries of all counties in England
are at present being reviewed for the purpose of straightening out
anomalies and abolishing detached areas. While these adjustments
are likely to be numerous for some while to come, they have not so far
involved the transference of many rainfall stations from one county to
another. Details of any necessary changes in the General Table have
been given in Part I of earlier volumes. Jn the case of the General
Table, Part III, of this volume 3 changes have been made for this
reason. The stations involved are given below and the changes have
also been indicated by footnotes in the General Table :
Station.
Little Gaddesden (Ringshall End)
Caversham (Ranmore) ...

Warwick 4
The opportunity has been taken to examine the allocation of
stations to the various river divisions in each county. In this case
revised watershed lines have been used as adopted by the Catchment
Boards established under the Land Drainage Act of 1930. This has
necessitated changes in this volume in 41 cases and again the alterations
have been noted by footnotes.
XJi BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
LOCAL ORGANIZATIONS. For assistance in the collection of
the rainfall returns for the year, and for stimulating local interest in
weather records, we are indebted to officials of various local
organizations, among whom may be mentioned : Mr. C. P. Sinclair,
of the Hertfordshire Natural History Society and Field Club ; Mr.
R. H. Primavesi, of the Northamptonshire Natural History Society ;
Col. H. C. Copeman, of the Norfolk Rainfall Organization ; Mr. A. P.
Jenkin, of the Cornwall Rainfall Association ; Mr. R. Burrow who
continued the work of Mr. A. P. Jenkins in Jersey ; Miss Cordelia
Marshall, of Ambleside ; Librarian, North Devon Athenaeum ; Mr.
W. Dunbar, Burgh Surveyor of Kilmarnock ; Mr. E. W. M. Murphy,
of the Irish Rainfall Association and Mr. E. L. Kelting who replaced
Mr. Goodman as Secretary of the Mid-Wessex Rainfall Association.
The Editors of the Hereford Times and the Western Morning News
have also been good enough to arrange for the printed summaries of
rainfall records to be available in time to be used for the same purpose.
Records published in the Surrey Advertiser have been examined and
those not received direct from the observers have been copied and
included in the General Table.
Mr. S. L. Rogers, J.P., of Williton, Somerset, has collected local
records for publication in the West Somerset Free Press and certain
of these records were also not received direct and have been included
in the General Table. Capt. W. N. McClean has lent rain-gauges
in areas in Inverness-shire and Ross-shire in connection with his work
on river-flow records and so provided additional records. The City
Engineer and Surveyor of Nottingham has continued to publish an
annual report on the Meteorology of Nottingham, which has provided
additional records. The Surrey County Council have also arranged
for local authorities to instal recording rain-gauges in connection with
work on drainage and flood.prevention.
Mr. C. P. Sinclair contributed a Report on the weather in Hertfordshire for the year 1938 to the Hertfordshire Natural History Society
and Field Club. Mr. Sinclair collects the rainfall data at the end of
each year frcfm the observers and compares the values with those
furnished to this Office in order that the two reports shall be in
agreement. Mr. C. P. Sinclair retired during the year from the post
of general manager and secretary to the Colne Valley Water Co., after
28 years service in that capacity. Mr. F. K. Sinclair has been appointed
chief engineer and principal officer and has already shown his interest
in the meteorology of Hertfordshire.
Mr. R. H. Primavesi contributed a Report on the weather in
Northamptonshire for the year 1938 to the Journal of the Northamptonshire Natural History Society along lines similar to those for Hertfordshire. The original records are forwarded to this Office for com-

Col. H. C. Copeman, Secretary Norfolk Rainfall Organization,
has continued to collect rainfall records from about 35 stations in
Norfolk each month and the daily values have been forwarded for
publication in the Eastern Daily Press.
Mr. A. Pearse Jenkin, Secretary, Cornwall Rainfall Association,
has continued the collection, monthly, of rainfall reports from about
60 stations and issued summaries on typewritten sheets to this Office
and others interested. The totals have been used in preparing the
monthly maps and the sheets examined for any new records.
Mr. R. Burrow, Engineer and Manager of The Jersey New Waterworks Co., Ltd., has continued the work of Mr. A. J. Jenkins and
forwarded the daily values for stations in the island to this Office at
the end of the year.
Miss Cordelia Marshall has continued to publish monthly a
summary of the rainfall experienced at 22 stations in the English
Lake District. The records from these stations were plotted on the
monthly maps.

The Librarian, North Devon Athenaeum, has forwarded each
month a list of monthly rainfall totals for some 15 stations.
Mr. W. Dunbar, Borough Surveyor at Kilmarnock, has continued
to collect records monthly for 30 stations in Ayrshire and surrounding
districts. The monthly totals are typewritten and circulated to
those interested, together with a map showing the distribution of the
rainfall over the area. These values have been plotted on the
monthly maps.
Mr. E. W. M. Murphy, Secretary of the Irish Rainfall Association,
has devoted himself during the last 30 years to the task of fostering
interest in rainfall observations in Ireland and to the establishment
of rain-gauges where new records were desirable. A summary of the
records has been received through Mr. Nagle, Director of the Meteorological Service, Eire. The annual report giving totals for 1937 was
published in October, 1938, and circulated to subscribers.
With the retirement of Mr. F. B. Goodman, Mr. J. E. Harben
and later Mr. E. L. Kelting forwarded the actual returns to this
Office monthly, after the copy giving the monthly totals had been
prepared for the Western Morning News. Mr. E. L. Kelting was
previously Engineer to the Cheshire Rivers Catchment Board, where
he had set up a number of additional rainfall stations, and now becomes
Engineer to the Somerset Rivers Catchment Board, and Secretary
of the Mid-Wessex Rainfall Association.
Xvi BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
Sincere thanks are due to the numerous observers, including many
water engineers, who have agreed, on being asked to do so, to forward
returns monthly instead of annually. The Organization cannot,
unfortunately, handle 5,000 or more rainfall returns monthly ; it
was therefore necessary to limit the number of stations to those which
were needed for a satisfactory geographical representation. We
shall continue to plot the annual totals from all stations and to use
all available returns in the general work of the Organization. The
co-operation of the great body of observers who are asked to send returns
only at the close of the year is therefore as valuable, and as much
appreciated as ever.
THE STAFF OF OBSERVERS. The detailed account of the
rainfall of the year 1938 over the British Isles, which is presented
in this volume, follows the lines of previous issues. The basis of the
discussion is a collection of about 5,700 returns of rainfall measurements for the year received from all parts of the country. The
measurements are generally made every day, but in inaccessible
districts they may be made only once a week or even once a month.
The Meteorological Office relies almost entirely on the returns contributed either by private persons, who measure the rainfall primarily
as a matter of private interest, or by public local authorities, and
especially the authorities of water undertakings, who find that a
knowledge of the rainfall in their locality is indispensable to them in
their work. These observers forward copies of their observations
voluntarily for preservation by the British Rainfall Organization of the
Meteorological Office as national records, and for use not only in the
preparation of the annual volumes of British Rainfall but also for the
purposes of study and research. In this way records which are
primarily of local interest acquire a national value which was not
always realized by those who initiated them. To the numerous
voluntary observers who forward their observations the Organization
desires to acknowledge its indebtedness : it would be quite impossible
to compile such an account as this without their assistance and goodwill.
The total number of records printed in Part III of this volume is
5,683, an increase of 112 records over last year. As many as 364
additional records are included in the volume, while 252 records
printed in British Rainfall, 1937, are no longer available. Details
as to the numbers of records printed in earlier volumes are given
In a separate table. The counties with the largest number of net
gains in England are Yorkshire W.R., Kent, Surrey and Cumberland
with 20, 10, 9 and 8 respectively.In Yorkshire there are 10 additional
records from Mr. G. E. V. Boldry (Engineer to the Dewsbury and
Heckmondwike \V.\V.), and 4 records from Mr. G. F. A. Atkinson
(Fylde Water Board). There is a gratifying increase in the number
RAINFALL RECORDS, 1938.
of rainfall stations in Scotland. Inverness-shire shows a net gain of
eight which includes four stations in the Loch Ness area and three
in the Spey Valley. The largest county loss was in Dumbarton where
five gauges in Glen Finlas were discontinued. In Ireland the number
of reporting stations has been maintained. Mr. R. W. Eaton, Engineer
to certain Catchment Boards in Lancashire and Mr. A. J. M. O'Byrne,
drainage engineer to the Lancashire County Council have been
responsible for a number of additional records in Lancashire. Reference
may also be made to a record of duration of rainfall received from
Kirkwall in the Orkney Islands.
Rainfall records for 1938 and for earlier years have been received
in a number of cases, including seven records from the Fylde Water
Board at stations on the moors above Settle and Slaidburn extending
in four cases back to 1917 and in one to 1912 ; two records for the
years 1934 to 1937 from Messrs. Balfour Beatty & Co., Ltd. from the
stations at Bruar Lodge above Blair Atholl in Perthshire ; at Ardee
in Co. Louth by Capt. R. A. B. Filgate since 1913; at Rudgwick
near Horsham by Mr. S. D. Secretan since 1918; at Waltham-onthe-Wolds to the north of Melton Mowbray by Mr. G. Higgens since
1918 ; at Heckmondwike (The Bottoms) by the Spenborough U.D.C.
since 1922 ; at Worcester (Samuel Southall School) by Mr. B. Brotherton since 1926; at Faversham Sewage Works by Mr. Colin Walton
since 1928, and at Fort Augustus Public School since 1929.
AVERAGES PRINTED IN THE GENERAL TABLE OF
BRITISH RAINFALL. Since 1926 a column in the General Table
has been devoted to the average annual rainfall at the various stations,
and year by year additional values have been given. This information
has been supplied mainly by the Rainfall Survey which is being carried
out county by county as the opportunity occurs. In the present
volume additional averages are included for a number of stations
used in connection with The Surface Water Year-Book of Great Britain,
1936-37. Additional averages have been received recently from Mr.
H. P. Cornish for stations in Surrey, which will be included in the
next volume.
INVESTIGATIONS. An article is printed in Part IV on the
distribution of the rainfall over the British Isles during the dry years
1854 to 1858. The subject is of some importance, especially in connection with schemes for the maintenance of water supplies, in that
this period gives the driest two, three four and five consecutive years
over the country generally. It is satisfactory to note that the investigation has resulted in the preparation of maps for these early years
for which little information has previously been available.
Dr. J. Glasspoole contributed an article on " The Rainfall of the
British Isles, a review of the development of our knowledge," to the
500th number of Water and Water Engineering.
XVlii BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
A paper on " The Seasonal and Geographical Distribution of
Absolute Drought in England " was contributed by Miss L. F. Lewis
to the Royal Meteorological Society in May 1939. The paper gives a
list of the absolute droughts recorded at Durham (1857-1937),
Rothamsted (1854-1937), Oxford (1854-1937), Camden Square (1858-
1937), Stonyhurst College (1848-1937), Liverpool Observatory (1846-
1937), Woburn (1895-1937), Totland Bay (1886-1937), Newquay
(1893-1937).
A paper on " The Trend of Annual Rainfall in Scotland " by
Dr. A. H. R. Goldie and Mr. H. E. Carter, was presented at the summer
meeting in Edinburgh of the Institution of Water Engineers.
Dr. J. Glasspoole was appointed to attend the meetings of the
Inland Water Survey Committee as Assessor on behalf of the
Meteorological Office in succession to Mr. E. G. Bilham.
INSPECTIONS. Some progress was made during the year with
the inspection of the stations included in the General Table. It was
possible to inspect about 280 stations, exclusive of Climatological
stations and other stations reporting general meteorological observations. In some cases faulty exposures were corrected and old gauges
were replaced by new ones of standard patterns, while in many other
cases it was possible to confirm that the exposures conformed to modern
standards. The stations inspected included those maintained by the
water supply undertakings at Burnley and Stockport ; those reporting
to the Lancashire County Council, and a number in Somersetshire.
Some 25 stations were inspected by the staff in Edinburgh.
SURVEY MAPS. The work on the detailed average annual
rainfall map of the British Isles on the scale of two miles to the inch,
for the period 1881-1915, has been continued. In drawing these
maps all the available information is used, averages for stations
with short records being weighted to the standard period 1881 to
1915 by comparison with nearby stations with complete records.
The averages so computed are included in Part III of British Rainfall.
Reduced copies of specimen maps and a description of the method
used in their preparation are given in articles published in Part IV
of British Rainfall 1928, 1929 and 1932. In drawing these maps the
isohyetal lines are naturally somewhat generalised, and if attention is
directed to any small area some amplification of the lines there may prove
necessary. Moreover, additional records are always being accumulated
so that slight modifications in the lines may subsequently prove
necessary. The isohyetal lines have been extended to cover Peebleshire
and certain small areas for the purposes of enquiries.
INQUIRIES. A large number of inquiries regarding the rainfall
of various parts of the country was dealt with. These enquiries cover
RAINFALL RECORDS, 1938.
a wide range. They include the supply of records denning the rainfall
experienced in some locality for a specified period, the supply of
estimates of the average annual or monthly rainfall for specified places
or over certain areas, and advice as to the most suitable instruments
and the best exposures for the rain-gauges. Amongst the reports
supplied reference may be made to those for the Nottingham Corporation Water Department, Messrs. Binnie, Deacon and Gourley in
connection with the rainfall over part of the River Severn Catchment
area, Messrs. Lemon and Blizard for the years 1932 to 1938 over the
Aled area (Rhyl U.D.C.), the County Council of Southampton on the
drought of 1938, and Messrs. Spence, Sales and Bland on the general
problem of drought in rural districts.
Information has also been supplied for use in The Surface Water
Year-Book of Great Britain, 1936-37, published by the Ministry of
Health and Scottish Office (H.M.S.O.). In the case of each area for
which run-off data are available, monthly totals October, 1936, to
September, 1937, are given for selected rainfall stations, together
with evaluations of the corresponding general monthly rainfall over
the area and the average amounts, where that information is available.
Monthly totals were included for stations representative of 71 areas.
In 24 of these areas the monthly and annual average value of general
rainfall were computed, and in 16 cases the monthly values for the
seasonal year 1936-37 were also given. In addition values for the
year 1935-36 were worked up for 5 areas. The methods use in the
computations were defined in the report on " The Determination
of the General Rainfall over any area " published by the Institution of
Water Engineers in 1937. The publication of rainfall and run-off data
together is likely to prove of particular value to those concerned with
the use of water.
Information has been supplied monthly to the Bristol W.W.,
the Humber Conservancy Board, the British Sugar Corporation, Ltd.,
the Lee Conservancy and the I.C.I. (Alkali), Ltd. Reports were
supplied monthly to the Metropolitan Water Board and to the Thames
Conservancy.
Data have been supplied monthly to the Catchment Boards of the
Rivers Mersey and Irwell, River Avon (Bristol), River Severn, Cheshire
Rivers and River Nene. Advice has been given on the distribution of
stations in the case of certain Catchment Boards.
OBITUARY. During the twelve months 1st July, 1938, to
30th June, 1939, reports have been received of the deaths of 71
observers who have contributed to volumes of British Rainfall. Of
these, 15 have kept rainfall records, or been associated with relatives
who have kept records for 30 years or more. A list of these is given
below. In most cases the records have been maintained throughout
by the same observer.

Mr. C. L. Brook, who died on May 9th 1939 in his 84th year,
commenced a rainfall record at Harewood Lodge, Meltham, Yorkshire,
in 1881 and a second record on the moors at Royd Edge in 1891.
His rainfall record at Harewood Lodge covers therefore over 58 years.
In addition earth thermometer records were forwarded for publishing
in the Meteorological Record of the Royal Meteorological Society
from 1899 to 1911 and general meteorological observations were
published in the Monthly Weather Report from 1912 up to date.
Mr. Brook was one of the Trustees of the British Rainfall Organization
from 1910 until it was taken over by the Government in 1919. The
following comments are quoted from Dr. Mill's article in British
Rainfall, 1909, p. 36, " It appeared to me that the first Trustees of the
British Rainfall Organization should be chosen from amongst the
rainfall observers, especially from those who were intimately acquainted
with the work of the organization .... The nine Observers who have
joined me as Trustees are known to most of my readers .....
Mr. C. L. Brook was an old friend of Mr. Symons, and one of the
most liberal supporters of the Rainfall Organization ; he represents
in a special sense the north of England." With the death of Mr.
Brook we lose one of our few remaining links with G. J. Symons and
the pioneers in the study of British Rainfall.
Mr. John Laughton, J.P., died on February 25th 1939. In
1897, when factor on the Eallabus Estate in Islay, Mr. Laughton
continued the rainfall observations which had already been maintained
there for some years. Later at Logan House, Wigtownshire, and
from 1930 at Corstorphine, Edinburgh, he also maintained records,
thus being responsible for observations covering a period of 42 years.
Mr. B. T. Rowswell commenced rainfall records in 1894 at St.
Martin's, Guernsey, and for many years collected records from the
Channel Islands for transmission to the Meteorological Office.
BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938
PART II
RAINFALL AND METEOROLOGY
1938
BRITISH RAINFALL, 193H.
1. A BRIEF SUMMARY OF THE RAINFALL OF THE
YEAR, 1938.
The outstanding features of the rainfall of 1938 were the severe
drought of February to April in parts of England and Wales and the
frequent periods of heavy and persistent rains in Scotland, which
resulted in abnormally large annual totals in the Western Highlands.
The rainfall of 1938 over the British Isles as a whole was 109 per
cent, of the average, so that the remarkable run of wet years, which
commenced in 1923, and lapsed only in 1933 and 1934, was continued.
A comparison of the rainfall of the nine years 1930 to 1938 is set
out below.
Annual Rainfall as per cent, of average, 1881-1915.

The comparison shows that whereas in 1936 and 1937 the smallest
percentages occurred for Scotland, in 1938 Scotland gave the largest
value. The year 1938 ranks among the five wettest years in Scotland
since 1870, the only wetter years being 1872, 1877 and 1903 with 134,
131 and 129 per cent, respectively.
The main feature of the map showing the distribution of the
rainfall as a percentage of the average was the large area in the
Western Highlands of Scotland with more than 150 per cent. So
large an area with over 150 per cent, has not occurred on any map
since that of 1903, when there was more than 150 per cent, over a
large part of the Thames Valley. At Inveraray Castle, in Argyllshire,
the total of 143-84 inches was 66-62 inches or 86 per cent, in excess
of the average. This percentage of 186 ranks as the largest on record
for any individual station.
The rainfall of February to April, 1938 over England and Wales
was most unusual, being only 2 2 inches, less than that in any similar
period back to 1785, when the general rainfall is estimated to have
been 2-1 inches. The general rainfall over the British Isles during
April only amounted to 0-4 inch. The smallest values previously
recorded in any month since 1870 were February, 1932 with 0-5 inch,
SUMMARY OF THE RAINFALL OF THE YEAR. 3
and February, 1891, June, 1925 and March, 1929 each with 0-6 inch.
Droughts were unusually frequent during 1938 and more widespread
than in any year since 1887. At Ross-on-Wye a partial drought
persisted from February 1st to May llth, a period of 100 days with
only -97 inch.
The rainfall of the three months October to December, with 14-5
inches over England and Wales, stands out in sharp contrast with the
meagre total of 2-2 inches for February to April. Owing mainly to
the abnormal sequence of wet weather over Wales, Scotland and
Ireland in October and November, the total of these two months over
the British Isles, viz. 12-7 inches exceeded that of any similar period
since before 1870, although October to November, 1929 gave 12-6
inches. Rain-spells were more frequent than usual during 1938, and
lasted for as many as 77 consecutive days, from October 2nd to
December 17th, at both Mull and Poltalloch. Since 1903 there has
been only one larger rain-spell at the selected stations.
The largest monthly total recorded during 1938 was 50-03 inches
for March at Loch Quoich (Loan). There are only three other monthly
totals of 50 inches or more on record, viz., October, 1909, with 56-5
inches at Snowdon (Llyn Llydaw) January, 1872, with 50-1 inches at
Borrowdale (The Stye) and January, 1928 with 50-0 inches also at
Borrowdale (The Stye).
In April there was no measurable rain at all over numerous
small areas in Southern Ireland and in parts of Hampshire, Dorset,
Cornwall and Pembroke.
Among the largest daily falls of rain during the year the following
may be mentioned :
Buttermere (Hassness) Cumb'land 7-14 in. July 29th.
Torquay (Abbey Gardens), Devon 5-61 in. August 3rd.
Kinlochquoich, Inverness-shire 5-35 in. February 3rd.
East Overton House, Lanarkshire 5-10 in. August llth.
The distribution of the rainfall of 1938 stands out in sharp contrast
with that of the previous year. A comparison of the rainfall over the
British Isles during the two years for the periods January to May,
and June to December is given below, together with the average
values :
January. May June-December
in. in.
1937 21-9 21-2
1938 13-2 31-9
Average 15-4 26-0
In 1937, January to May gave as much rain as occurred during June
to December, whereas in 1938 the last seven months gave nearly
two and a half times as much as that of the first five months.
BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
2. THE DISTRIBUTION OF RAINFALL IN TIME.
THE NUMBER OF RAIN-DAYS AND WET-DAYS IN 1938.
The following definitions have been used in British Rainfall in
order to obtain a homogeneous series of statistics :
A rain-day is a period of 24 hours, commencing normally at
9 a.m., on which -01 inch, or 0-2 mm. or more of rain is recorded.
A wet-day is a period of 24 hours, commencing normally at
9 a.m., on which -04 inch, or 1 -0 mm. or more of rain is recorded.
The former definition dates from the year 1865, the latter was
adopted only about 1917. Each of the terms " rain-day " and
" wet-day " is open to criticism, the latter particularly so, since it has
a different meaning in ordinary use. It must, therefore, be understood
that whenever these terms are used in this publication the meanings
set out above are always to be attached to them.
For reasons which are set out in detail in the article entitled " The
distribution over the British Isles in time and space of the annual
number of days with rain," in British Rainfall, 1926, there is frequently
a lack of harmony in the annual values at adjacent stations. Statistics
of wet-days are found to be much more consistent than those of
rain-days. It was this reason, coupled with the fact that similar
data are published for certain other countries of Europe, which led
to the introduction of statistics of wet-days. The satisfactory mapping
of the annual values of rain-days has always presented considerable
difficulties, whereas the values of wet-days have proved sufficiently
consistent to enable the distribution to be defined. Maps showing
the distribution of wet-days have been included in British Rainfall
since 1919. The experience gained in drawing these- maps of wetdays annually has been utilised in interpreting the average values of
rain-days as recorded at certain stations during the period 1881 to
1915. Maps showing the distribution of annual and monthly average
number of rain-days over the country were published in the Quarterly
Journal of the Royal Meteorological Society, 1928, pp. 89-101, but
similar maps of wet-days have not yet been prepared.
Information as to the number of rain-days and wet-days during
the year is set out in this volume in various forms. As usual, the
General Table of rainfall in Part III of this volume shows the number of
rain-days and wet-days in 1938 as reported from each station at which
daily observations were made. The number of wet-days have been
plotted on a large map of which the illustration on p. 5 is a reduced
copy. The distribution of the numbers is shown on the map in the
usual manner.
THE DISTRIBUTION OF RAINFALL IN TIME.
In order to study the distribution of rain-days and wet-days
throughout the year, 100 stations have been selected to represent as
uniformly as possible the conditions over the whole of the British
Isles. The numbers of rain-days and wet-days recorded at these
WET-DAYS 1938

stations are given in Table V, and the same stations are used in the
preparation of Table VIII, Droughts and Dry Spells, and Table XI,
Rain Spells and Wet Spells. The monthly totals for these 100 selected
stations are included in Tables XX and XXI. The stations are the
same as those used last year except that additional stations at
6 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
Ravenglass (The Grove), Lochruttan (Dumfries \V.\V.) and Limerick
(Mulgrave Street) are used instead of those at West Witton, Dumfries
(Crichton R. Institute) and Mallow (Longueville). Monthly general
numbers (for England and Wales, Scotland, Ireland and for the
British Isles) have been evaluated and they are shown in Table V
in heavy type. The annual mean values for 1938 are shown again
in Tables I and III and the monthly mean values for 1938 in Tables II
and IV, where in each case they can be readily compared with similar
values for the intervening years from 1917, and with the averages.
In the case of rain-days averages are available for the standard period
1881 to 1915, but for wet-days the averages cover only the period
1917 to 1938.
The distribution of the annual number of wet-days during
1938, as shown on the map on the previous page, is very similar
to that for earlier years. As in all the other maps there is a steady
increase in the number of \vet-days from the south-east to the northwest of the British Isles and from the plains of the east coast to the
mountainous regions of the west. In 1938 the number varied from
about 100 in the neighbourhood of the Thames Estuary and along the
main river, the Wash, and in parts of Bedfordshire and Cambridgeshire to 250 at a number of stations in the mountainous regions in the
north-west of Scotland and at one station in Connemara. Most
stations in the south-eastern half of England recorded less than 125
wet-days but elsewhere this number was confined to the east coast of
Great Britain and the neighbourhood of Dublin.
The total number of rain-days varied from about 150 in the
neighbourhood of the Thames Estuary and the Wash, to about 280
in parts of the north-west of Scotland.
The general values given in Table III indicate that the frequency
of wet-days was about the average for the period 1917 to 1938. The
number for 1938 was 6 less for England and Wales, 12 more for Scotland,
6 more for Ireland and 1 more than the average for the British Isles
as a whole.
It should be emphasized that in the case of wet-days comparable
statistics are only available back to 1917, and the averages used for
comparison are for the period 1917 to 1938, which includes mainly
wet years.The averages are therefore greater than they would have
been if statistics covering the standard period 1881 to 1915 were
available. This fact is brought out by Table I, which shows that the
mean annual number of rain-days during the twenty-two years, 1917
to 1938, was 209 or 5 more than the average for the standard period
1881 to 1915.
The number of wet-days over the British Isles in each month is
given in Table IV. The numbers vary from 3 in April to 20 in January
and October. The general number of 3 in April is 10 less than the
average and the smallest recorded in April since comparable records
THE DISTRIBUTION OF RAINFALL IN TIME. 7
became available in 1917. There are only three other months with
as few wet days, viz., June 1925, March 1929 and February 1932.
The total number for the three months February to April was only
20, or 17 less than the average. This is the smallest recorded in
three consecutive months, the next smallest being 21 in February to
April 1929. October and November, with 20 and 19 wet-days respectively, each gave five more than the average numbers. The total
number for October, November and December was 56 and this has only
been exceeded once in a similar period, viz., October to December
1929 with 60 wet days. August stands out again as a month of
relatively few wet-days. In fact the mean for the seven years 1932
to 1938 is 11 for August, compared with 13 for both July and
September, a state of affairs markedly different from the normal.
In dealing with the general numbers of rain-days given in Tables
I and II, it is possible to compare the values for 1938 with computed
averages for the 35 years, 1881 to 1915. It is shown in Table I that
over the British Isles as a whole there were 208 rain-days or 4 more
than the average number. The number of rain-days was 6 less than
the average over England and Wales, 18 more over Scotland and 13
more over Ireland.
The number of rain-days over the British Isles in each month
is given in Table II. The range is from 6 in April (9 less than the
average) to 24 in January and October (5 more than the average in
each case). The number in April, viz., 6, was less than in any previous
April back to 1917, the next smallest number in April being 11 in
1921. The total for the three months February to April was 31,
or 19 less than the average, and less than in any previous three consecutive months since 1917, the next smallest number being 32 in
February to April 1929. October, November and December together
gave 68 rain-days, the largest number since October to December
1929 with 71 rain-days.
During the year the total number of rain-days over the British
Isles was 208 ; of these, 162 received sufficient rain to rank as wetdays. There were 3 more rain days than wet days in both April and
August, and 4 more in the remaining months.
Fourteen of the 100 selected stations failed to record a wet-day
during April and two of these failed to record a rain-day, viz., Poole,
in Dorsetshire, and Waterford. Ross-on-\Vye failed to record a
rain-day during March, and the total number of rain-days for March
and April together was only 2. Ross-on-Wye recorded no rain at
all during the 34 days February 27th to April 1st and during the 19
days April 3rd to 21st. (See Section 3.) Some rain fell on every day
during November at four stations, viz., Poltalloch, in Argyllshire,
Mull, Delphi Lodge, in Co. Mayo, and Crom Castle, Co. Fermanagh,
and at Delphi Lodge each day ranked as a wet-day.

DROUGHTS AND DRY SPELLS. 15
3. ABSOLUTE DROUGHTS, PARTIAL DROUGHTS AND DRY
SPELLS IN 1938.
As in the analogous case of rain-days and wet-days (Section 4),
certain definitions have to be adopted in order to obtain a reliable
body of statistical information on the subject of droughts. The
definitions used are :—
An absolute drought is a period of at least 15 consecutive
days, to none of which is credited -01 inch of rain or more ;
A partial drought is a period of at least 29 consecutive
days, the mean daily rainfall of which does not exceed -01 inch ;
A dry spell is a period of at least 15 consecutive days to none
of which is credited -04 inch of rain or more.
These terms are used in British Rainfall only in the senses stated.
More concise definitions of two of these terms would be :—An
absolute drought is a period of at least 15 days, none of which is a
rain-day ; a dry spell is a period of at least 15 days, none of which
is a wet day. The definitions of absolute drought and partial drought
were introduced in British Rainfall, 1887, page 21, while that of dry
spell was first used in British Rainfall, 1919, page 15.
Details are given in Table VIII of the absolute droughts, partial
droughts and dry spells, which occurred at the 100 selected stations
of Table V. The monthly totals at these selected stalions are included
in Table XX. Stations from which no droughts wtre reported are
omitted from Table VIII.
During 1938 the number of stations out of the 100 which failed to
record either a drought or a dry spell was only four, a number much
smaller than usual, though in 1934, 1932 and 1929 the numbers were
nine, four and one respectively.
In Tables VI and VII the total number of droughts and dry
spells recorded at these 10'.' stations in 1938 are compared with
those of each year from 1917 to 1937. For absolute and partial
droughts similar information is available as far back as the famous
dry year 1887 and averages are therefore available for the 52 years
1887 to 1938. Statistics, comparable with those given in Table VI,
are given for the years 1887 to 1916 in British Rainfall, 1929, page 20.
16 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
The total number of absolute droughts recorded in 1938 was 108
or 47 more than the average 1887 to 1938. The number of partial
droughts was 98 or 44 more than the average. The number of dry spells
was 171 or 33 more than the average. The number of absolute
droughts, viz., 108, has only been exceeded since 1911 on two occasions,
1917 and 1929, with 109 and 110 respectively. It was appreciably
less, however, than in the famous dry year 1887 with 160. Of the
100 stations only 6 had no partial drought and 7 no dry spell. Partial
droughts were more widespread than in any year since records became
available in 1887. although the corresponding number in 1932 was 7.
Dry spells were more widespread in 191 1, 1929 and 1932 when only
5, 1' and 5 stations respectively failed to record any.
The longest absolute drought lasted 38 days from April 3rd
to May 10th at both Port William, Wigtownshire, and Limerick. Since
iccords became available in 1887 only one longer absolute drought
has been recorded at the selected stations, viz., 44 days, from March 6th
to April 16th, 1893, at Towcester, Northamptonshire, although an
absolute drought of 38 days also occurred from June 9th to July 19th,
1921, at Patcham, Sussex. The longest partial drought persisted from
February 1st to May llth, a period of 100 days, at Ross-on-Wye.
Again the only year with longer partial droughts was 1893 which
gave Hailsham, Dorking and Corfe Castle with 114, 112 and 112 days
respectively from the beginning of March to about June 21st. The
longest dry spell lasted for 48 days from March 22nd to May 8th, at
Newcastle-on-Tyne. This number was exceeded in the recent years
1921, 1929 and 1932 when the longest dry spells covered 58, 53 and 51
days respectively.
The two diagrams show the total number of absolute droughts
and dry spells during 1938. In the north-western half of Scotland only
Tiree recorded an absolute drought. The absolute droughts were
confined to the period February 27th to May llth, except that stations
in the Isle of Wight and Hertfordshire also recorded an absolute
drought from June 2nd to June 26th. As many as 77 out of the 108
absolute droughts occurred within the period March 26th to May llth.
The partial droughts were confined to the period February 1st to
May 26th with the exception of a partial drought from June 1st to
July 2nd at Camden Square (London) and at St. Peter Port, Guernsey.
The dry spells were more uniformly distributed throughout the year.
Four dry spells occurred at Ross-on-Wye, although at Shoeburyness
and Derby dry spells printed in British Rainfall, 1937 were continued in
1938. In such cases the entries are given in italics.

24 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
4. RAIN SPELLS AND WET SPELLS IN 1938.
The definitions of the terms " rain spell " and " wet spell," as
used in this section, are analogous to those of the terms " absolute
drought " and " dry spell," which are used in the previous section.
Thus :—
A rain spell is a period of at least 15 consecutive days to
each of which is credited -01 inch of rain or more.
A wet spell is a period of at least 15 consecutive days to
each of which is credited -04 inch of rain or more.
In other words, a rain spell contains at least 15 consecutive
rain days, and a wet spell contains at least 15 consecutive wet days.
Table XI contains particulars of all rain spells* and wet spells*
during 1938 which were reported from the 100 selected stations of
Table V. Stations from which no rain or wet spells were reported
are omitted from this table. The number of stations so omitted
is 32, 24 in England and Wales including the Isle of Man, 6 in Scotland
and 2 in Ireland. Tables IX and X give comparative statistics concerning the rain spells and wet spells since 1917. Similar details of
rain spells are available back to 1903, and are given in British Rainfall,
1929, p. 30, and averages are therefore available for the 36 years,
1903 to 1938.
In 1938, the total number of rain spells at the 100 stations was
203 or 36 more than the average. The total number in 1938 has been
exceeded, since comparable statistics became available in 1903, in only
six years, viz., 1903, 1910, 1916, 1923, 1927 and 1934 with 282, 235,
231, 222, 206 and 206 respectively. The mean duration of the rain
spells was 22 days, one day more than the average. The mean rain
* There is a difficulty connected with the identification and enumeration of rain spells which
arises from the fact that spells may be proceeding both at the beginning and end of the year under
review. In regard to the latter, it has to be provisionally assumed (in the absence of definite information) that all spells terminate at the end of the year. If a spell which was in progress at the end of
1937 is now found to be continued into 1938, full details of the whole spell appear in Table XI. These
entries are printed in italics, and they can be regarded as corrections to the provisional entries made
in British Rainfall, 1937. In Tables IX and X the columns headed " number of spells " do not include,
in the entry for 1938, spells which commenced in 1937, and were counted as spells in British Rainfall,
1937, although they include spells, which commenced near the end of 1937, but were not of sufficient
duration in that year to qualify for admission to the 1937 table. Those columns dealing with the
mean duration take account, however, of that part which occurred in 1938 of those spells which
commenced in 1937, and were counted in that year. If a spell commenced in 1937, of which the
duration in that year was insufficient to count as a spell for that year, the mean duration in Tables
IX and X refers to the whole spell, including the portion which actually occurred in 1937. On the
other hand, a spell which commenced in 1937 is considered to be eligible for notice under " greatest
duration," if it terminated in 1938, even if it has already been counted as a spell in 1937. (These
conventions were first printed in British Rainfall, 1926, p. 25.)
RAIN SPELLS AND WET SPELLS. 25
per day during the rain spells was -29 inch, -02 inch more than the
average. At both Mull and Poltalloch, in Argyllshire, a rain spell
was recorded lasting 77 days from October 2nd to December 17th, with
mean amounts per day of as much as • 48 inch and • 35 inch respectively.
Since 1903 only one longer rain spell has been recorded at the selected
stations, viz., 89 days, August 12th to November 8th, 1923, at Islay
(Eallabus), but on that occasion the mean rain per day was only
-31 inch. Rain spells occurred during 1938 at 67 stations, two less
than the average.
As many as five rain spells during the year were recorded at 13
stations. There were seven at both Fort William and Buncrana
Rectory (Co. Donegal).
The total number of wet spells at the 100 stations was 70, or
21 more than the average. This has only been exceeded twice since
1917, viz., in 1928 and 1930 with 87 and 71 respectively. The mean
duration of the wet spells was 20 days and the mean rain per
wet day -40 inch, both values being slightly in excess of the average.
Wet spells occurred at 36 stations or 8 more than the average. Three
stations, viz., Poltalloch and Mull in Argyllshire and Loch More
(Achfary) in Sutherlandshire, recorded as many as five wet spells
.and this is the largest number of stations with five since records
commenced in 1917.
The longest wet spell occurred at Delphi Lodge, Co. Mayo, and
lasted from October 23rd to December 9th. This gave 48 days with
28-84 inches.
Of the 70 wet spells 3 occurred in England, 6 in Wales, 41 in
Scotland and 20 in Ireland. The number for Scotland and Ireland
together of 61 is exceptional and compares with 78 and 56 in 1928 and
1930, respectively, the years with the largest numbers of wet spells
over the country generally since 1917.
The rain spells and wet spells were mainly confined to the first
month of the year, and to the period from the end of September to the
end of the year.

34 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
5. DURATION OF RAINFALL IN 1938.
The duration of rainfall is obtained from measurements of the
records of autographic gauges. On these records the amount of rain
is measured vertically and the duration of the rainfall is measured
horizontally, in accordance with scales which are printed upon the
charts before they are placed in position in the gauges. The pen
of an autographic gauge writes a horizontal line on the record during
such time as no rain is falling, and it writes a more or less sloping
line whenever rain is passing into the receiver of the gauge.
The duration of rainfall discussed in this section is denned as
the length of time during which rain is falling at a rate not less
than 0-1 mm. per hour, i.e., -004 in. per hour. The tabulations at
all the official stations are made according to this convention, which
was first brought into general use in British Rainfall, 1920. It was
adopted because during very light rainfall it was found to be almost
impossible to decide upon the exact point on the record where the
change from " no rain " to " rain " occurred. By taking a definite
limiting slope of the trace as the criterion this difficulty is, to a great
extent, avoided and it is found possible to obtain comparable measurements from different gauges and from different observers. The
limit of 0-1 mm. (-004 in.) per hour is low enough to ensure that
no fall of practical importance is excluded.
The list of stations reporting duration of rainfall for 1938 is
given in Tables XII and XIII. These tables have been arranged
in this volume so as to give information of the total duration of
rainfall and of the mean rate of rainfall for the same stations at one
opening. The distribution of the stations for 1934 was shown on a
map on p. 42 of British Rainfall, 1934. The list includes records from
120 stations. Amongst the records published for the first time,
reference may be made to those from Worthing and Bodle near
Hailsham, the only stations in Sussex ; those from three additional
stations in Cheshire, supplied by the Cheshire Rivers Catchment
Board ; that supplied by the University Observatory at Durham, and
that from Kirkwall in Orkney. The distribution of the stations is
more satisfactory than in previous years, but there are still large
areas unrepresented, especially in Ireland and the northern half of
Scotland.
Complete records from several stations were obtained from the
Dines Tilting Syphon, which is now being adopted as the standard
Meteorological Office pattern recording rain-gauge. A diagram showing
the principle of the gauge is given in British Rainfall, 1936, p. 35. A
description of the gauge also appears in the 1934 edition of the
Meteorological Observer's Handbook, p. 126, and in the Meteorological
Magazine, 1935, pp. 237-8.
DURATION OF RAINFALL. 35
Hourly total values of the duration of rainfall, and totals for the
days and months commencing at midnight, for the four Observatories
Kew, Eskdalemuir, Aberdeen and Valentia, are to be found in the
Observatories' Year Book.
Table XII sets out the duration of rainfall expressed in hours
at 120 stations in the British Isles, for each month and for the whole
year of 1938. For the purposes of this table the month commences
either at 7 a.m. or 9 a.m. G.M.T. on the first day of the month and ends
at the same hour on the first day of the following month. The station
with the highest aggregate duration for the year is Glenleven (Blackwater Dam) with a duration of 1,996 hours. This period is equivalent
to a total of about 83 days, or just under 5| hours per day throughout
the year. The only other stations with aggregates reaching or exceeding
1,000 hours were Greenock, Eskdalemuir, Snowdon, Pontsticill, Cray
Reservoir, Valentia, Kirkwall and Kirriemuir with 1,365, 1,358, 1,317,
1,081, 1,057, 1,019, 1,018 and 1,000 hours respectively. Twelve
stations recorded 350 hours or less, mainly situated in the Midlands,
east and south England and including Port on (on Salisbury Plain),
Felixstowe, Kingston-on-Thames and Nottingham.
The month of least duration was April over most of the British
Isles. In the east of England, however, several stations from Felixstowe
to Manston gave June as the month with least duration, while at
Ross-on-Wye and Moretonhampstead March was the month of least
duration. Totals of less than 10 hours were recorded by six stations
in February, 34 in March, 95 in April, 31 in June and 2 in July. The
total was " nil " for Ross-on-Wye in March and Poole in April ; and
numerous stations had only one hour of rain during April.
The month of longest duration was January in coastal districts
of the south-west of Ireland, Wales and England, but December
gave the largest duration over most of England, Ireland and eastern
Scotland. The month of longest duration was October in the northwest of England, on Snowdon and over much of Scotland. At stations
in the Western Highlands of Scotland March gave the longest duration,
and if recording gauges had been in operation in the neighbourhood
of Loch Quoich March would have given there the longest duration,
since as much as 50 inches was recorded (see Section 8). The only
months during which no station reported as many as 100 hours of
rainfall were April and August. In April the largest total was only
49 hours at Glenleven. Values exceeding 200 hours were confined to
this station where the totals in January, March, October and November
were 255, 276, 227 and 247 hours respectively.
Details of the duration of rainfall are available at Camden Square
(London), back to 1881. Two periods during the year call for special
comment. The six months February to July gave a total duration
of only 80 hours, the smallest ever recorded for this period. The

44 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
previous smallest total for February to July was 98 hours in 1921.
The total in December of 127 hours was the largest for December,
117-5 and 110-5 hours being recorded in the Decembers of 1914 and
1934. In fact, December, 1938, gave the largest total for any month
with one exception, viz. March, 1916 with 134 hours. The total for
the three months October to December, 1938, of 252 hours exceeded
that of any similar period in the record, although the duration January
to March, 1916, amounted to 255 hours.
Table XIII sets out the mean monthly and annual values of rate
of rainfall at each of the stations of Table XII. The rainfall amounts
are taken from the ordinary gauge for making daily observations
which is set up alongside the recording gauge. The values are obtained
by dividing the total fall for the month or year by the duration as
given in the preceding table. The mean rates for the year vary from
•104 in. at Snowdon (Cwm Dyli), and -098 in. at Princetown, to
•041 in. per hour at Kirriemuir. The highest monthly mean rates
were -179 and -167 inch per hour at Lympne for June and Cork for
April. At Lympne -39 inch fell in 5 minutes on the 1st and the total
duration for June was only 8 hours. The mean rates recorded during
December were smaller than usual in that month, owing to falls of
snow during the period 19th to 26th.
HEAVY FALLS IN SHORT PERIODS. 45
6. HEAVY FALLS IN SHORT PERIODS IN 1938.
Heavy falls of rain in short periods are of considerable economic
importance, for they are frequently of such intensity as to cause
extensive flooding, often accompanied by much surface erosion and
structural damage to property. Considerable damage may occur as
a result of the accumulation of flood water below the area of heaviest
rainfall. The amount of the damage depends on a number of other
factors, such as the shape of the catchment area, the nature of the
ground and vegetation, whether the water finally flows into the river
or sea at high or low tide and the relative positions of the towns.
In towns the floods may be aggravated by the failure of the drainage
system to cope with the demands made upon it. The information
given in this section supplements that in Section 7, Heavy Falls on
Rainfall Days, which deals with falls of longer duration causing often
more widespread flooding.
Since 1908, this section of British Rainfall has contained a
classification of intense falls into three categories designated " noteworthy," " remarkable," and " very rare." The classification has
been effected by reference to a diagram of which a copy was reproduced
on p. 44 of British Rainfall, 1935. This diagram indicates by continuous curved lines the lower limits of rainfall which must fall in
stated times in order that the fall may be admitted into one of the
three categories. The diagram was prepared almost exclusively
from eye observations of rainfall duration and from corresponding
measurements of rainfall in ordinary rain-gauges.
On pp. 45-46 of British Rainfall, 1926, it was pointed out that
the frequency of intense falls above the curve denoting the limit of
noteworthy falls was in actual fact much greater for small intervals
of time (i.e., the left-hand side of the diagram) than for larger intervals
(the right-hand side of the diagram^. Consideration was therefore
given to the question of modifying the curves so that they might
represent, as nearly as possible, rainfalls having a uniform frequency
of incidence. A new classification-diagram was prepared, full
particulars being given in an article in Part IV of British Rainfall, 1935.
The diagram is reproduced on p. 47 and the lower limits are set
out in the table on p. 44. These limits were brought into operation
on January 1st, 1936.

A list of the recorded falls of " very rare " intensity of one hour
or less, qualifying for inclusion under the new classification, is given
in Table XIV. There are five additional entries for 1938, viz., at
Torquay and Paignton for August 4th, Lexden for August 6th,
Chichester for August 7th and Symington, in Ayrshire for August 10th.
In Table XV particulars are given of falls recorded in 1938 which
reached at least the intensity " noteworthy." Unlike Table XIV,
falls of longer duration than one hour are included, the upper limit being
two hours. In general not more than one entry is made for the same
shower for any station. The main entry gives the largest amount
recorded with a mean intensity " noteworthy," " remarkable,"
or " very rare," as the case may be, an entry at the higher rate being
in general preferred. In the case of entries ranking as " very rare "
additional entries are occasionally included in italics under " noteworthy " and " remarkable," and in the case of " remarkable"
entries under " noteworthy" also where such information is
available. In Table XV additional information was available in only
one case. Unless the amount was -43 inch or more, intense falls of less
duration than five minutes are not included in the table, since in such
cases the error is likely to be relatively large and such falls are not
usually of practical importance. The entries are credited to the
calendar day rather than the " rainfall day."

Most of the entries occur in south-east and south-west England.
There are only five entries for Scotland and none for Ireland. The
total number 73 compares with 86 in 1937 and 65 in 1936 and was
seven in excess of the mean for the years 1925 to 1938. Usually
about 80 per cent, of the total number of intense falls occur in the
three months, June, July and August. In 1938 as many as 64 of the
73 entries occurred in July or August, of these 60 occurred during the
first twelve days of August. The number in south-west England and
Wales was 28 or nearly twice the mean.
The only intense fall for May occurred in the afternoon of the
17th at Cranwell Aerodrome during a thunderstorm, when 1-12 inch
fell between 17h. 15m. and 18h. 21m. of which -98 in. fell in 48 minutes.
A waterspout was noted to the south-west at 17h. 15m.
Thunderstorms occurred on June 1st in Kent. At Sellindge,
near Ashford, 1-40 in. occurred between 14h. 45m. and 15h. 45m. and
at Elham, near Folkestone, 1-20 in. fell in two hours. Mr. J. H.
Dyson, of the Old Vicarage, Preston near Canterbury, reports that,
while no measurement of the maximum intensity was made, probably
•85 in. to -90 in. fell between 16h. 30m. and 16h. 50m. Torrential
hail continued while the sun was shining. Floodwater collected to
a depth of 2 to 3 in. on level ground around the houses, within
15 minutes of the start.
Heavy falls occurred during July on the 1st and 7th. On the
1st at Wolverley, near Kidderminster, out of a total of 1-62 in. one
inch fell between 18h. and 19h. Severe hail occurred which lay like
snow and did not melt entirely for 24 hours. At Derby 1-47 in. fell
between 14h. 45m. and 16h. 30m. in a thunderstorm with rain and
hail. At Market Harborough the following details are sent by Mr
P. J. Harris :—
" From 16h. 20m. to 18h. 15m., two thunderstorms were more or
less simultaneously in progress here, accompanied by intense rainfall.
One storm, approaching from the south-west, preceded the other,
HEAVY FALLS IN SHORT PERIODS. 49
from the east-north-east, by some ten or twelve minutes, and the
former was virtually overhead from 17h. 20m. to 17h. 25m., while
the nearest approach of the later storm was about 1 mile away to the
east at about 17h. 55m. Hailstones of moderate size, diameter
1-0 to 1-5 cm., fell for several minutes during the maximum period.
From about 17h. 55m. to 18h. 05m. the sun shone brightly through
breaks in the clouds and a rainbow was noticed. Shortly after the
cessation of rain, I measured the amount of rainfall recorded in my
5 in. rain-gauge, the amount being 1-95 in. The following morning
my garden showed the force of this heavy rainfall in various ways ;
a flat portion of the kitchen-garden had the clear pressed appearance
of the sea-beach after the tide has receded ; some rows of potatoes,
' moulded up,' were washed nearly flat and one potato ' set ' was
lying exposed above the ground ; small seedlings of wallflowers,
etc., were washed out of their seed-beds ; and across a sloping portion
of the kitchen-garden channels 3 or 4 inches deep and several yards in
length were cut by the rushing streams, while the hollows at the edge
of a lawn and of grass-paths were choked with several inches of finelywashed sediment. A remarkable feature of this intense downpour
of rain is the fact of the restricted area upon which it fell. My house
is on the extreme eastern boundary of the town, on a hillside 356 ft.
above sea-level. Abour three-quarters of a mile westwards, at the
offices of the Urban District Council, the rainfall read at 9h. was
0-75 in."
On the 7th -75 in. fell in 20 minutes at Alton (Wyck).
The intense falls mentioned in Table XV for August were confined
to the first twelve days, when a remarkable series of widespread
thunderstorms giving intense rains locally was experienced. There
was a period of 13 consecutive days of more or less widespread thunderstorms as recently as June 14th to 26th, 1933 (see QJ.R. Met. Soc.
59, 1933, p. 416) but the storms of 1938 were of a severer type, occurring
in warmer and damper weather. "Remarkable" falls occurred on
August 1st, 1938, at Winsford, Winscombe and Williton, all in Somerset.
At Williton 1-25 inch fell between 15h. 30m. and 16h. in a severe
thunderstorm. On the 2nd at Tenby, in Pembrokeshire, 2-15 inches
was recorded the bulk of which fell during a thunderstorm between
14h. and 17h. 35m.
The most striking rains of August occurred during the morning
of the 4th. At Torquay as much as 5-72 inches fell in five hours and
ten minutes, between 5h. 50m. and llh. of which 4-25 inches occurred
in 108 minutes from 6h. 12m. to 8h. The diagram gives the best
indication available as to the incidence of the rainfall and is based on
the autographic record supplied by Mr. C. Bellinger, in charge of the
meteorological station at Torquay, who was there by 6h. 30m. He
had to wade through water knee deep and Belgrave Road was a
river torrent. Details are given in Table XV of falls ranking as

Mr. E. F. Duggan, B.A., of Holmwood House School, Lexden, writes:
"' the rainfall in the deluge of Saturday, August 6th, was 2-25 inches
and this fell between 16h. 30m. and 17h. 30m. when the thermometer
showed a fall of 18 degrees from 83°F. to 65°F." This fall ranks as

Mr. Henry Mousley writes from Corringham, near Stanford-le-Hope :

sultry afternoon and travelled towards the north-east with hardly
any wind." Of the two falls in Lancashire that at Liverpool ranks
as "remarkable." The City Engineer reports that 1-14 inch fell
between 6h. and 6h. 24m. Mr. J. Whiteby, of Broadway (Buckland
Manor) reports that 1-77 inch occurred between Ih. and 3h. during a
violent thunderstorm accompanied by hailstones of unusual size.
Of the entries for Sunday, August 7th, three are from Hampshire
and one from Sussex. Mr. G. N. Stanley Beale writes from Crawley
(Chilbolton Down) : " On August 7th as much as 1-95 inch occurred
between 15h. 15m. and 16.h 45m., and although of exceptional intensity
the rain was not accompanied by thunder." The entry ranks as
" remarkable." At Southampton (Civic Centre) one inch was
recorded between 14h. 30m. and 15h. 5m. The rain continued until
19h. when a total of T42 inches was measured. The storm took
the direction Lymington, Lyndhurst, Totton, Southampton, Lee-onSolent and Ryde, I.O.W. Mr. S. J. K. Eames, of Apuldram, near

2-50 inches from 15h. 15m. to 16h. and notes that most of the 3-06 inches
measured for the day fell by 16h. 30m. Heavy thunder occurred

and a squall of wind blew from south-west for the last part of the time.

The only entry for August 8th was from Manston Aerodrome in

The entries for August 9th are widely distributed being in
Northamptonshire, Wiltshire, Devonshire, Cheshire and Lancashire.
Those at Wallasey, St. Helen's, Chope Barton and Bishops Cannings
rank as " remarkable." At Wallasey 1-80 inch fell between 14h. 25m.

13h. and 14h.
Of the four entries for August 10th that for Ayrshire ranks as
" very rare." Mr. Thomas McMillan of the School House, Symington,
near Kilmarnock, reports that -72 inch fell in 20 minutes from 15h. 30m.
to 15h. 50m. After an interval of ten minutes, when the gauge was
emptied, the storm was renewed sharply at 16h. and lasted until close
on 17h. when it ceased almost as suddenly. The gauge gave 2-44 inches
for the second period, making a total of 3-16 inches for the 90 minutes,
15h. 30m. to 17h.
Of the eleven entries for August llth, four are from Middlesex,
two from Surrey, and one each from Lanark, Renfrew, Kent and
Devonshire. Those at Wembley and Glassford Filters, near
Strathaven, rank as " remarkable." At Wembley, Mr. L. Petre
reports that 2-17 inches occurred in 90 minutes. Mr. J. Monger
writes : " During a severe storm at Wembley on Thursday, August 11th,
which lasted from 17h. to 18h. 30m., 2-5 inches of rain fell. Some of
the roads became rivers. Motorists were stranded, the carburettors
of their cars being flooded, and trolleybuses were put out of action.
Certain roads became miniature lakes 3 feet deep and most of the
cinemas were flooded. Owing to the flooding and lightning striking
cables there was a big telephone breakdown. On Friday, August 12th,
a heavy storm occurred in the afternoon at Mill Hill and Hendon
(which adjoins Wembley) and there was extensive flooding. A house
was struck by lightning. Hailstones the size of moth balls fell and
leaves of water lilies in a fishpond at Moat Mount Golf Club were
pierced. In some parts the rain gauges recorded 1-5 inch of rain,
while other areas in the district had no rain at all." At Sudbury,
near Wembley, 1-39 inch fell between 17h. and 18h. 30m. At
Stonebridge Park 1-48 inch fell in 115 minutes from 16h. 35m. to
18h. 30m., of which 1-28 inch occurred in the 45 minutes 16h. 45m. to
17h. 30m. At Purley, Surrey, -95 inch fell between 16h. 21m. and
16h. 53m. At Edenbridge, Kent, 1-21 inch occurred from 16h. to

and 2-26 inches, respectively, the whole of the latter amount occurring
in a second thunderstorm from 16h. 15m. to 18h. 30m. At Moretonhampstead, Dartmoor, -91 inch occurred from 14h. 50m. to 15h. 10m.
At Glassford Filters, near Strathaven, 2-5 inches fell between 15h. 30m.
and 17h. 30m. ; further details are given in section 7.
There are thirteen entries for August 12th and those at Palmers
Green, Peterborough and Edenbridge rank as " remarkable ". At
Palmers Green the heavy rain occurred about 12h. 30m. At Peterborough the thunderstorm caused considerable damage. Mr. E. H.
Colman recorded 1-18 inch in 20 minutes about 14h., the peak of the
storm being from 13h. 45m. to 14h. Two miles away men were
carting hay until 16h. : although they expected the black clouds to
give rain earlier. At Edenbridge (Gilridge) 2-09 inches fell between
16h. and I8h. At Knap Hill, near Woking, 1-00 inch occurred
between 16h. 30m. and 17h. 10m. At Haughley, near Stowmarket,
three centres of thunderstorms appeared to approach from the north,
north-east and south-east about 15h. 55m., when heavy rain continued
until 17h. The rain was so heavy that the observer could not see
across the road. At Warminster the heavy rain occurred between
13h. 25m. and 15h. 15m. when the measurement was 2-12 inches.
The last four months of the year give only two entries. That
for October 12th at Maesteg, Glamorganshire, of 1-57 inch between
12h. 17m. and 13h. 8m. ranks as " remarkable." The last entry
for the year was at Kilmington, near Axminster, on November 25th.

The falls are not necessarily continuous nor are they always confined to one calendar day. Not more than one fall of each specified amount
is credited to any day ; if more than the specified amount fell on any day.that part of it which fell in the shortest time is used for the table. An
entry in any of the first four columns under the heading 10 mm. (0-4 inch) naturally recurs in the column or columns to the right, and similarly with
the entries under headings 15 mm. (0'6 inch), 20 mm. (0'8 inch) and 25 mm. (TO inch).
HEAVY FALLS ON RAINFALL DAYS. 57
7. HEAVY FALLS ON RAINFALL DAYS IN 1938.
The daily measurements of rain which are set out in this Section
are (a) those of falls of 2-5 inches or more in the rainfall day, and
(b) those of falls which amount to at least 7-5 per cent, of the total
recorded for the year 1938. A fall of 2£ inches in a day at any station
is sufficiently remarkable to be noticed, but as such heavy falls are
more frequent in the normally wet areas of mountain districts than
elsewhere, the criterion of 1\ per cent, was introduced to admit
certain falls of less than 2| inches in areas where the total rainfall
for the year is less than 33-3 inches. In this way daily falls which
are heavy in relation to the local standard are not neglected.
A summary of the information given in this section in each of the
seventy volumes of British Rainfall from 1865 to 1934 was set out in
an article in Part IV of British Rainfall, 1934. A complete list of the
absolute maximum daily rainfall and the absolute maximum daily
percentage reported for each year from 1865 to 1934 was also given
in this special article.
The corresponding values for 1935 to 1938 are given below.

The maximum daily amount for 1938 of 7-14 inches and the
maximum percentage for 1938, viz., 15-4 are not remarkable, since
larger entries have occurred in many recent years.
Particulars of the seven largest falls of the year 1938 are given
in Table XVIII. The table shows also the year's total at each of the
stations (where this is available) and the percentage of that total
which is accounted for by the highest daily fall. There were 52
falls reaching or exceeding 4-00 inches and 26 falls reaching or exceeding
4-50 inches. The total number of falls exceeding 2-50 inches was 420
58 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
and exceeding 3-00 inches 190. The number of falls reaching or
exceeding 2-50 inches in 1938 was appreciably greater than in the
previous four years but less than in 1933, 1932, 1931 or 1929. The
number of falls reaching or exceeding 3-00 inches in 1938 was surpassed
only in 1931 and 1929 within the same period. There were as many
as 114 falls of four inches or more in 1931.

CHRONICLE.
The heavy falls recorded in this section for January were confined
to stations in the English Lake District and the Western Highlands
of Scotland.

Details of the severe gale, especially in the west of the country, caused
by the deep depression on the 14th and 15th are given in the Meteorological Magazine, 1938, pp. 4-9. The front along the trough on
the 15th was apparently a new development, probably due to convergence in unstable air, associated with the turn towards the east
HEAVY FALLS ON RAINFALL DAYS. 59
in the path of the centre. It is of interest to note that on this
occasion the completely developed system travelled some 700 miles
across the Atlantic, whereas in the general case, severe and widespread
gales are associated with active secondary disturbances which develop
relativel}T close in to the British coasts and then move quickly across
the country. The extreme velocity registered during the gale on
the 15th was a gust of 101 m.p.h. at St. Ann's Head, Pembrokeshire.
Around the coast of Wales, Lancashire and Cumberland the gale
was coincident with some of the highest spring tides. At Aberystwyth
extensive damage was caused by the high seas along the front, and
in the west of Cumberland a railway embankment was washed away.
The total at Watendlath Farm, to the south of Keswick, for the
four days 12th to 15th amounted to 5-23 inches, and for the five days
12th to 16th 6-67 inches.

At Loch Quoich (Kinlochquoich) the total for the four days
18th to 21st amounted to 7-78 inches, for the fourteen days 18th to
31st to 19-79 inches, and for the eighteen days January 18th to
February 4th to 31-59 inches.
A map showing the distribution of the rainfall for February 3rd
over part of Scotland is reproduced. The interesting feature of the
distribution is the sharp contrast between the area in the east with
60 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
Rainfall, 3rd February, 1938.
no rain and the large totals in the Western Highlands Practically all
stations in England, Wales, Ireland and the south of Scotland reported
no measurable rain and there was no rain at Tiree in the extreme
west of Scotland. Most of the country was under the influence of
an anticyclone centred over Central Europe but an intense secondary,
to a depression south of Iceland, moving rapidly to the north-west of
Scotland, gave heavy orographical rains. Under these conditions
the pressure gradient in the north-west of Scotland was much steeper
than usual and strong south to south-west winds prevailed. This
type of rainfall distribution in Scotland has occurred under similar
conditions on a number of occasions, of which those of December 9th,
1909, and October llth, 1916, may be quoted. On the latter occasion
as much as 8-20 inches was recorded at Kinlochquoich on Loch Quoich,
while there was less than half-an-inch over much of the eastern half
of Scotland.
The synoptic weather chart of 13 h. on the 3rd is reproduced.
It is interesting to note that Aberdeen had a temperature of 56°F.
compared with 44°F. at Wick. This was due in part to the Fohn
effect at Aberdeen and partly to a stationary front along the isobars.
The heavy rain occurred apparently in a narrow belt near the front,
in those regions where the orography was favourable. A separate
warm front crossed the country during the night and was responsible
for the continuation of the rain.

The rainfall of the 25th and 28th February was associated with
the passage of depressions to the north-west of the British Isles
accompanied by gales in the north and west. The total rainfall at
Kinlochquoich, Inverness-shire, for the four days the 25th to 28th
amounted to 7-06 inches.
The heavy falls for March were not individually striking. The
occurrence of falls worthy of mention in this section on as many as
nine days in the north-west of Scotland is, however, unusual and
resulted in unusually large totals for the month in the Western
Highlands. The total at Loan, at the head of Loch Quoich, v/as
50-03 inches (see Section 8), but this station is so far from any habitation that daily values are not available. At Loch Quoich, Kinlochquoich, the total for the nine days, 23rd to 31st, amounted to
20-25 inches, and the eleven days March 23rd to April 2nd to
25-35 inches.

It is noteworthy that the entries for June are confined to
mountainous areas in the west of the country. This distribution is
associated with the strong west winds which were a feature of the
weather of the month. An article on The Gales of June, 1938, was
published in the Meteorological Magazine, 1938, pp. 139-142. Over a
considerable part of southern England the gale on the night of June
1st to 2nd attained a violence unprecedented for the time of year
since systematic wind measurements began. At Calshot a gust of
88 m.p.h. occurred about 2h. 40m. on the 2nd.

The rains of June 26th and 28th were accompanied by depressions
with gales almost as severe as those of the beginning of June. On the
26th a complex system travelled across the Atlantic without much
development. During the 27th, whilst the system crossed Scotland,
the pressure at Lerwick fell to 968 mb. at Ih. on the 28th, an abnormally low value for June. Widespread gales occurred and a gust of
77 m.p.h. was recorded at Aldergrove, in Northern Ireland. On the
28th another complex disturbance, which had formed further west
on the same frontal system, crossed the British Isles. The deepening
of this disturbance took place throughout its rapid journey across the
Atlantic. A feature common to all these depressions was the
complexity of the frontal structure in the early stages. As mentioned
previously the exceptional disturbances of June gave heavy rain in
the west. They afforded little relief from the dry conditions which
prevailed in south-east England, details of which are given in Section 3.

Isolated stations recorded heavy falls in July on the 1st, 3rd, 8th
and 27th, many stations in the south-east of Ireland on the 7th and
as many as 51 stations, mainly in the north-west of England, on the
29th. Mr. P. J. Harris gives details of the thunderstorms experienced
at Market Harborough on the 1st in the Meteorological Magazine,
1938, p. 178. Details of the intense rain experienced during this local
storm are given in Table XV. The heavy rains of the 7th were
associated with a depression which developed off south-west Ireland
on the 6th and moved across England and the North Sea by the
morning of the 9th. Details of hailstorms at Eastbourne and Bishops
Waltham, near Winchester, on the 7th are given in the Meteorological
Magazine, 1938, p. 179, including a photograph of the accumulation
of hailstones in the Square at Bishops Waltham.

July 27th

July 29th gave the largest daily amounts recorded during the year.
The totals for the 29th and 30th amounted to as much as 9-20 inches
at Buttermere (Hassness), 8-06 inches at Borrowdale and 7-45 inches
at Watendlath Farm. These stations are all in the English Lake
District but the total on Snowdpn for the two days was 7-30 inches.
The rainfall occurred in a pronounced south-westerly current, mostly
in the warm sector close to a slow-moving cold front, which was so
poorly denned that its position on a map can only be given approximately. This type of meteorological situation is favourable for heavy
rainfalls, especially in mountainous districts. Numerous examples
could be quoted, but perhaps the most outstanding of recent years is
that of November 2nd to 3rd, 1931, described in British Rainfall,
1931, pp. 67-73.
