July 30th
Carnarvon.—Snowdon (Llydaw Intake)...... .3-10 in. or 1-7 per cent.
In contrast with the rains of June and July, which were mainly
of the orographical type, the heaviest rains of August were mainly of
the thunderstorm type and details of their intensity have been given
already in Section 6. There is also an article on the " Thunderstorms
of August, 1938 " in the Meteorological Magazine, 1938, pp. 195-202,
which includes the following statement. " Really spectacular night
storms are always preceded by great heat at no great distance. As a
rule the air is maritime tropical in origin and is heated for a few days
over land. In some cases prolonged continued heating over the
land seems to be the cause of the great heat. In the case of the storms
of August it is impossible from the information at present available
to trace the air back previous to the appearance of very high temperatures over south Russia on July 23rd, with an east wind. Since
thunderstorm frequency is high in eastern Europe, it is evident that
the moisture is often there, and its occasional westward transport
presents no difficulty."
The distribution of the rainfall of August 3rd and 4th is shown
in the diagram. There was more than two inches over an area
stretching from Tintagel to Exmouth including Dartmoor and Exmoor.
More than four inches occurred from Torquay and Paignton to
Moretonhampstead and Two Bridges and the distribution over this
area is indicated in the diagram. The entries for Torquay (Abbey
63 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
Park) were 5-61 inches and -78 inch, a total of 6-39 inches. To the
west the totals were smaller, 3-97 inches being recorded at Ipplepen
Vicarage. Further north-west in the neighbourhood of Hedgebarton,
Ilsington and Ashburton the totals increased, the daily entries being
at Hedgebarton 5-86 inches and -59 inch, Ilsington 5-35 inches and
•65 inches, and Ashburton 3-79 inches and 1-28 inches, respectively.
The distribution of the heavy rain has some resemblance to
that which prevailed on July 17th, 1926, and on August 15th, 1905,
65432 I in
Rainfall, 3rd and 4th August, 1938.
although in these cases the areas of heavy rain were further east
and the totals not nearly so large as those for August 3rd to 4th, 1938.
To the south-west of Torquay heavier rain was also experienced. At
Stoke Gabriel (4 mi. to the south-west of Paignton) the amounts
were 5-08 inches and -91 inch, the total of 5-99 inches occurring from
6h. to llh. A copy of the autographic rainfall record at Torquay is
included in Section 6.

HEAVY FALLS ON RAINFALL DAYS. 71
Interesting features of this article are the photographs of a waterspout observed some eight miles to the south-west of the centre of
heavy rain and apparently closely connected with it. Mr. P. E. Kirton
of Dungavel, near Strathaven, noted " what appeared to be a trunklike structure of cloud shot out from near the edge of a nimbo-stratus
cloud " and the articles gives considerable details of the circumstances.
Flooding caused a landslide on the L.M.S. Railway at Strathaven, a
subsidence took place on the railway line from Stonehouse to Strathaven and there was extensive damage in the district. At East
Overton House precipitation from 14h. to 23h. amounted to 5-10 inches
" hailstones which came for about two and a half hours after 14h. 45m.
were the size of pullets' eggs." At Stonehouse 3 inches fell between
15h. and 20h., the most intense precipitation was between 15h. and
17h. and included a heavy fall of ice hail. The maximum precipitation
undoubtedly appreciably exceeded the actual measurements and
probably occurred somewhere in the gathering grounds of Muir Burn
and Priest Burn, to the east of East Overton House. The existence
of highly unstable conditions was vividly shown by the occurrence of
a waterspout, which probably marked the local beginning of the breakdown in the instability which was subsequently extended to form an
area of torrential rain.

Heavy falls of rain during September were confined to the later
half of the month, when cyclonic rains occurred. September 24th
was the wettest day at most stations in London, but August 7th or
November 25th were wetter at a few stations. At Camden Square
(London) the duration of rainfall amounts for September 24th and
27th were 10 and 11 hours respectively, the longest duration for any
72 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
day of the year apart from certain days in December. The heavy
storm of the 26th was confined to a small area, only one inch being
recorded at Bognor l|mi. to the east, but there is not sufficient
information to define its extent or duration.

Between the 2nd and 5th October depressions moved north-east
over Scotland, very unsettled weather prevailed with severe gale on
the 4th and widespread thunderstorms on the 5th. The disturbance
HEAVY FALLS ON RAINFALL DAYS. 73
which crossed Scotland during the early hours of the 4th was very
intense and caused considerable damage. From the 6th to the 13th
the tracks of the main depressions lay to the northward of Scotland,
while associated secondary depressions passed across the British Isles.
The totals recorded during this period at Borrowdale (The Moraine)
and Watendlath Farm, in an adjoining valley, were similar, those for
the second station being for the 2,5,7 and 11 consecutive days, 2nd to
3rd, 2nd to 6th, 2nd to 8th, and 2nd to 12th, 7-38, 12-23, 15-78 and
18- 75 inches respectively. At Cwm Dyli, at the foot of Snowdon, and
at Loch Quoich (Kinlochquoich), in the Western Highlands of Scotland,
large totals were also recorded, the corresponding values being 3-34,
6-15, 8-63 and 13-34 inches and 4-90, 7-60, 9-35 and 13-75 inches
respectively.
The rainfall amounts for the 2nd exceeded one inch over Snowdon,
the Pennines and area to the west, part of the Southern Uplands and
the greater part of Scotland from Argyllshire and Inverness-shire
to the west of Aberdeenshire, Angus and Fifeshire. Falls of more
than two inches were recorded near Loch Quoich, in Inverness-shire,
over the English Lake District, the Pennines between Settle and
Wensleydale, and near Darwen, in Lancashire. Some rain occurred
at most stations in the British Isles, but in the Midlands and Southeastern half of England and Wales, and in Ireland, the amounts were
small.

The area with more than one inch was much more widespread on
the 3rd than on the 2nd. On the 3rd more than one inch occurred
over Dartmoor, most of Wales, the Pennines and area to the west,
over most of the western half of Scotland stretching across the
Grampians almost to Aberdeen and over the Cheviots, over much of
the north-western half of Ireland and also the Mourne Mountains
and mountains of Kerry and Cork. The amounts exceeded two inches
at a number of stations in south and north Wales, the English Lake

District and parts of the Pennines, the Southern Uplands of Scotland

October 25th
Ross and Cromarty.—Glenshiel (Kyle) ............... .3-10 in. or 1 -5 per cent.
Heavy falls occurred in November for the 12th and 25th at a
number of stations, and on other days at a few isolated stations in
Scotland. Between the 5th and 15th an anticyclone moved north-east
from Spain to Central Europe and Russia, while Atlantic depressions
moved north-east or north along the west coast of Scotland. This
pressure distribution was associated with a flow of unusually mild
equatorial air over the British Isles for November. Maximum
readings of 70°F. were recorded on the 5th at stations in east and
south-east England. The totals for the 5th and 6th at Kinlochquoich
and Glenquoich amounted to 8-93 and 4-97 inches respectively.
The distribution of the rainfall on the maps for the 5th and 6th was
similar. In both cases measurable rain occurred almost entirely
only in the north-western half of Scotland, falls of one inch or more
were confined to mountainous regions north of Oban and two inches to
small areas on Ben Nevis and near Loch Quoich. The areas were
generally rather larger on the 6th than the 5th.

Inverness. in. or % Inverness (contd.). in. or %

The rainfall of the 12th was much more widespread over the
country, but heavy falls were mainly confined to the mountainous
districts of the west of Great Britain. There was no measurable rain
over most of the south-eastern half of England and most stations in
Ireland recorded less than half-an-inch. There was more than one
inch over most of \Yales, Westmorland, Cumberland, the Southern
Uplands, to the south of Edinburgh as well as from Perthshire and
Argyllshire to Ross-shire. As much as two inches were recorded on
the Pentland Hills, but the largest totals of three inches or more
occurred in South Wales, Snowdonia, the English Lake District and
on Ben Nevis.

The distribution of the rainfall of November 25th was entirely
•different from that which prevailed during the other days of November,
or October, which have been quoted, when orographic rains prevailed.
November 25th was the wettest day of the year in south London.
A feature of the weather of the 25th to 28th was the development of a
secondary depression in maritime polar air accompanied by heavy
rain in the south. Thunderstorms occurred early on the 25th both in
south-west England and Brittany. There was a diffuse frontal
zone, or a series of minor fronts, which became nearly parallel to the
isobars. With the development of the secondary, a single frontal
system became dominant. At 13h. on the 25th the secondary was
iorming to the west of Ireland and at 18h. became a developing centre
just to the south-west of Ireland. The rain area then covered the
whole of the south-west of the country and the front was to the north
of Scilly and the Lizard. The wind was already force 9 on the
Beaufort scale at the Lizard.

The distribution of the precipitation for the period 19th to 26th
over England and Wales is of special interest, although the amounts
were not large. The eastern half of the country was much wetter
than the western half during this period. There was more than one
inch to the east of a line drawn roughly from Beachy Head to include
most of Berkshire, the Chilterns, Cotswolds, the east of the Pennines
and the Cheviots. The totals exceeded two inches near Lincoln and
in the west of the county of Durham. To the south of London and in
the extreme north-east of England there was less than one inch.
Over most of the Devon-Cornwall Peninsula, over Wales and to the
west of the Pennines (except the Lake District^ there was less than
half-an-inch. It is not practicable, however, to define the proportion
of this precipitation which fell as snow, although most fell in that form.

8. MONTHLY RAINFALL IN 1938.
Information as to the rainfall of the months of 1938 is set out in
this section in various forms :—
(1) Table XIX (a) gives general values of the monthly and annual
rainfall over the British Isles as a whole and also over the constituent
countries separately.
(2) Details are given of some of the largest and smallest monthly
totals of rainfall reported during 1938.
(3) In Table XX the monthly and annual totals of rainfall are
set out for 400 stations, distributed as evenly as possible over the
British Isles.
(4) Table XXI gives the monthly and annual falls expressed as
percentages of the averages for 1881 to 1915. It is confined to
150 stations, uniformly distributed over the British Isles, for which
averages are available. The monthly totals in inches for stations in
Table~XXI are given in Table XX. At the end of Table XXI general
monthly and annual percentage values are given for the British Isles
and for each of the constituent countries.
(5) A set of twelve maps showing the distribution of the actual
rainfall in each month, is reproduced in this article. A thirteenth map
depicting the distribution of rainfall for the whole year appears in
Section 10. These maps were originally drawn on the much larger
scale of 16 miles to the inch,and values from the 2,000 stations reporting
monthly were plotted on each map before the isohyetal lines were
drawn. In 1938 a new base map was used being on the larger scale of
16 miles, instead of 19 miles, to one inch.
(6) A second set of twelve maps showing cartographically the
distribution of the percentage monthly values given in Table XXI,
as well as in the rainfall tables published month by month in the
Meteorological Magazine and in the Monthly Weather Report is also
reproduced in this article. The corresponding percentage map for the
whole year appears later in Section 10.
In Table XIX (b) the average values, taken from the Book of
Normals, Table 2lA (M.O. 236, Section V), are given for comparison
with the values for 1938 given in Table XIX (a).
80 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
General Monthly Rainfall.
The general monthly values shown in Table XIX (a) have been
computed by applying the percentage values given at the end of
Table XXI to the average general values for the period 1881 to 1915.
These computed values have been subsequently slightly adjusted in
order that the totals for the year should agree with the totals given
in Table XXIII which are based on a more exact determination as
explained in the text accompanying that table.
The wettest months over the country generally were October
and November with 6 • 6 and 6 • 1 inches respectively, and the driest
months April, March and February with only -4, 1-8 and2-0inches
respectively. October ranked as the wettest and April as the driest
over each of the main divisions of the country. The total rainfall
October to November, 1938, 12-7 inches, exceeded that of any similar
period since before 1870, the next wettest being October to November,
1929 with 12-6 inches. The total of the eight months May to December
amounted to as much as 35-3 inches or 85 per cent, of the annual
average, but this amount was exceeded in May to December, 1924 with
37-0 inches.
At the end of Table XXI the general values are given as percentages
of the average. The rainfall was less than the average over the British
Isles as a whole in only four months, viz., February, March, April
and August. April, 1938, with 18 per cent., was easily the driest
April since before 1870, the next driest April being that of 1893-with
35 per cent. October, 1938, with 159 per cent., was the wettest
October since that of 1903 with 193 per cent. It is noteworthy that
each January in the three years 1936 to 1938 has been very wet
and that 1938 gave the third wet July, the fourth dry August and
the fifth consecutive wet September.
The general rainfall over England and Wales during February,
viz., 1-2 inch, although small, appreciably exceeded that of the recent
Februarys of 1932 and 1934, with 0-5 and 0-6 inch respectively.
The rainfall of March, viz., 0-7 inch was the smallest value for March
since before 1870 apart from those of 1893 and 1929 with 0-6 and
0-3 inch respectively. The general rainfall for April amounted to
only 0-3 inch and, so far as can be ascertained, was less than that
in any April since before 1727. The only months in the series back
to 1870 drier than April, 1938 were February. 1891 and June, 1925,
each with a general rainfall of 0-1 inch. The sequence of three months
as dry as February, March and April, 1938 was most unusual. The
total amount February to April, 1938 was only 2-2 inches and less
than in any similar period back to 1785, when the general rainfall
is estimated to have been 2-1 inches. In the " famous spring drought
of 1893," the three months March to May gave a larger total, \iz.,
2-8 inches. In fact, February to April, 1938 ranks as the driest
three consecutive months on record since before 1870. At Camden

(a) Monthly Totals—Some of the largest monthly totals recorded
during 1938 are set out below :—

As much as 50 inches has been recorded in any month at any station
on only three previous occasions, viz., October, 1909, January, 1872
and January, 1928. (see Section 1).
On the other hand, no rain at all was recorded during April, 1938,
over 1,546 sq. mi. (786 sq. mi. in the south-west of England, 118 sq. mi.
in Pembrokeshire and 642 sq. mi. in the south-east of Ireland). The
only other months in which larger areas received no measureable rain
appear to have been February, 1891 and June, 1925, when the total
areas with no rain were 3,290 and 6,410 sq. mi. respectively. The
following stations recorded no rain during the month :—

MONTHLY RAINFALL. 83
Rainfall February to April, 1938.
The distribution of the total rainfall during February to April,
1938, over England and Wales calls for special comment. Over the
greater part of England the totals failed to reach two inches. More
than two inches occurred, however, to the north-west of a line from
Derby to Newcastle-upon-Tyne ; in the Devon-Cornwall peninsula ;
between Yarmouth, Norwich and Bury St. Edmunds and over the
greater part of Kent and the eastern half of Sussex. Totals of rather
less than one inch were recorded over a number of areas, viz., from
Retford, in Nottingham, to Leicester ; from Huntingdon to London
and extending westwards to the borders of Wiltshire and including
much of the valleys of the Thames and Lee ; in parts of Hampshire
and to the east of Wales, covering parts of Shropshire, Worcestershire,
Herefordshire and Gloucestershire. More than five inches was recorded
over part of Dartmoor, near Blackburn and in the Lake District.
Most of Wales received more than three inches. More than five inches
occurred in parts of Glamorganshire and over the mountainous districts
of central and north Wales. There was more than ten inches in the
neighbourhood of Lake Vyrnwy, in Montgomeryshire, and on Snowdon.
The Rainfall and Weather of the Individual Months.
*+ * Maps showing the distribution of mean pressure and the movements of
the centres of depressions for each month are published in the
Monthly Weather Report.
In the following pages maps are given showing the distribution
of the actual rainfall in each month, and of the percentage monthly
values, together with a short description of the weather of each
month. Further details are given in the Monthly Weather Report and
in the Meteorological Magazine.
BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
January.—The month was generally unsettled, mild and wet
\\ith frequent and sometimes severe gales and was noteworthy for
the absence of frosts in England and Wales. Although there were
no really heavy falls, snow fell frequently and over wide areas from
the Sth to 17th, on the 21st and from the 24th to 31st in Scotland.

BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
February-—The month was generally dry, except in the northwest of Scotland. As in January, the weather was mainly mild, but a
cold spell occurred in the middle of the month when snow was lying
for a few days in Kent.
Rainfall, February, 1938.
Mean pressure markedly exceeded the average generally, the
excess at 7h. ranging from 9-2 mb. at Kew to 11-9 mb. at Malin
Head. At Deerness, Orkneys, the range from 27-995 in. on the 1st
to 30-625 in. on the 19th was the largest ever recorded there in one
calendar month.

88 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.

varying from 1-2 mb. at Lerwick in the Shetland Isles to 13-0 mb.

greater part of Scotland and Ireland, but more variable over England
and Wales owing to the intense local falls. Less than 25 per cent,
occurred near Yarmouth and Montrose, but more than twice the
average in a number of small areas.

Rainfall, August, 1938, as per cent, of average.
The map of the rainfall in inches shows only a small area with
over 10 inches. The totals at Torquay and East Overt on House were
as much as 8-80 and 8-09 inches respectively.
100 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
September brought a few warm days, but a further deficiency of
sunshine. Mild conditions prevailed and continued until midDecember. The mean pressure at 7h. was slightly below the average
everwhere, except to the north of Scotland, the deviation varying
from —1-5 mb. at Birr Castle to +0-4 mb. at Lerwick.
SEPTEMBER, 1938
RAINFALL IN INCHES
Rainfall, September, 1938.
The total rainfall exceeded the average in the eastern half of
England, over Scotland generally and in the south-west and northeast of Ireland. More than twice the average was recorded in small
areas in Cambridgeshire and Essex, where local thunderstorm rains
MONTHLY RAINFALL. 101
occurred on the 24th and 27th. There was less than half the average
in parts of Radnor, north Devon and Hampshire.
At the Birmingham W.W. at Rhayader, the maximum daily
entry at the station at Tyrmynyddwas -36 inch, whereas at Chelmsford
2-32 inches fell on the 27th.
I
Rainfall, September, 1938, as per cent, of average.
On the map showing the distribution in inches the driest parts
of the British Isles were near Reading, Nantwich and Barnstaple,
where the totals did not reach 1 inch.
102 BRITISH RAINFALL, 193S.
October was remarkable for the stormy weather which prevailed
in the west and north. The mean pressure at 7h. was below the
average practically everywhere, the deviation ranging from —10-6 mb.
at Lerwick to +0-7 mb. at Scilly. The pressure gradient over the
country was considerably greater than usual.
OCTOBER, 1938
RAINFALL IN INCHES
Rainfall, October, 1938.
Less than the average occurred in the north-east of Scotland
and the south-eastern half of England, but few stations gave less than
75 per cent. There was more than twice the average over large areas
in central Ireland, western Scotland, north-west England and north
Wales. The percentages for Keswick and Inveraray Castle were 303
MONTHLY RAINFALL. 103
and 321. At Inveraray the total of 22-59 inches was as much as 29
per cent, of the annual average and more than one inch was measured
on 13 days. At Keswick, the total of 16-99 inches was 31 per cent,
of the annual average, and the seven days October 2nd to 8th gave as
much as 9-67 inches. At Watendlath Farm, to the south-east of

104 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
November was exceptionally mild with unsettled, wind and wet
weather. The gale on the 23rd was widespread and unusually severe
in England, Wales and Ireland. As in October, the mean pressure
at 7h. was below the average generally, the deviation varying from

Rainfall, November, 1938.
— 11-6 mb. at Lerwick, to —3-1 mb. at Yarmouth. The pressure
gradient over the country was again steeper than usual.
The general distribution shown on the percentage map for
November was similar to that for October. The totals exceeded
the average in most parts of the country, but small deficiencies
occurred in east Kent, the neighbourhood of the Wash, at Llandudno

average in parts of Dartmoor, south-west Wales, from the English

106 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
December was noteworthy for the continuation during the first
half of the month of the unusually mild weather of the autumn and
the severe cold spell with snow from the 17th to 26th. The mean
pressure at 7h. slightly exceeded the average in the north and was

Rainfall, December, 1938.
somewhat below the average in the south, the variation being from
+2-4 mb. at Lerwick, to —2-6 mb. at Kew.
The distribution shown on the percentage map for December
was markedly different from that for the previous two months. In
December, the largest values occurred in the east and south-east
MONTHLY RAINFALL. 107
of the land masses and the smallest values in the north of Scotland.
At Nairn there was as much as -04 inch on only 8 days, the largest
amount being -22 inch on the 6th. At Boston there were 20 wet
days, and -58 and -64 inch occurred on the 21st and 31st respectively.
Reference is made to the heavy precipitation, mainly in the form of

December most of the country recorded between 3 and 6 inches.

126 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938
9. SEASONAL RAINFALL, 1937-38.
There are definite advantages in considering the rainfall of the
winter and summer separately. One defect of the calendar year
I i
SEASONAL RAIN FALL 1937-38
WINTER HALF-YEAR
OCTOBER 1937- MARCH 1938
SO 100
Rainfall Winter Half-year, as per cent, of average, ^j
as a unit of time for rainfall purposes is that part of a winter is
included in one calendar year and the other part in another year.
SEASONAL RAINFALL. 127
Winter rainfall has a special economic importance, for, except in
the wettest areas, the summer rainfall is, for the most part, lost by
evaporation or absorbed by vegetation.
In this section the rainfall of the winter season, 1st October,
1937, to 31st March, 1938, and of the summer season, 1st April,
SEASONAL RAINFALL 1937-38
SUMMER HALF-YEAR
Rainfall, Summer Half-year, as per cent, of average.
1938, to 30th September, 1938, is dealt with, as well as the rainfall
of the whole seasonal year, 1st October, 1937, to 30th September,
1938. It is natural to associate a summer season with the preceding
rather than with the following winter season, since the water required
128 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
in summer for replenishing streams and for the needs of animal and
vegetable life is derived, in part, from that which has been naturally
stored during the previous winter season. One section of British
Rainfall has been devoted to this subject since the volume for 1908,
and three maps and Table XXII similar to those introduced in that
volume, are now reproduced.
SEASONAL RAINFALL 1937-38
YEAR
OCTOBER 1937 - SEPTEMBER 1938
0 _50 _ 100
SCALE OF MILES.,
Rainfall, Seasonal Year, as per cent, of average.
Table XXII contains rainfall values for each of these two seasons
as percentages of the average fall for the season and the station. The
table gives in the fourth column the corresponding percentages
SEASONAL RAINFALL. 129
for each station for the whole of the seasonal year. These last figures
are not always the mean of the percentages for the two seasons, but
often show a bias towards the winter value rather than that of the
summer. This is due to the fact that the average rainfall for the
winter is, in general, greater than that for the summer.
The 150 stations of Table XXII are as far as possible the same as
those used in British Rainfall, 1937. The monthly percentage values
for these stations are given in Table XXI and the monthly amounts in
inches are included in Table XX, where values are given for 400
stations.
The winter half-year included five dry months. The general percentage values for the British Isles for the six months October 1937
to March 1938 were :—78, 52, 89, 150, 63, and 57 respectively.
The general rainfall for the whole country for the winter half-year
was 18 per cent, less than the average, the deficiencies over England
and Wales, Scotland and Ireland being 19, 16 and 23 respectively.
Over England and Wales the rainfall of the winter half-year 1937-83
was less than in any similar period since 1906-07 except those of 1908-09
1920-21, 1931-32 and 1933-34 with 73, 77, 75 and 74 per cent, respectively. Over Scotland there were only two drier winters, viz., those
of 1908-09 and 1933-34 with 77 per cent, in each case. Over Ireland
only one winter was drier, viz., that of 1933-34 with 71 per cent.
The rainfall of the winter half-year was less than the average
over most of the British Isles. More than the average occurred in
parts of Durham, Yorkshire N.R. and in the west of Scotland. The
largest percentage values were recorded at Inveraray Castle, Argyllshire, and Loch Quoich (Glenquoich), Inverness-shire, viz., 124 and
111 per cent, respectively. Many stations recorded less than 70
per cent, and these were situated near Farnham, Brecon, Bala, in the
north-west of England, near Cromarty, to the south of the Wicklow
Mountains, near Killarney and over a large area from Co. Mayo to
Co. Meath. The smallest percentage values were 61 at Appleby,
and 62 at both Stonyhurst College and Carlisle.
The total rainfall for January to March at certain stations only
reached 3 inches. Small totals were recorded for the winter half-year
amongst these were 8-97 inches at Grantham, 9-11 inches at Nairn,
and 9-65 inches at Oxford. The largest total at the stations in
Table XXII was 78-09 inches at Loch Quoich (Glenquoich) in Inverness-shire.
The slimmer half-year included the very dry April; May, June
and July with moderate excesses ; August with slightly less than
the average, and September with a small excess. The general rainfall
for the British Isles as a whole was 103 per cent. ; England and Wales
had a deficienc}T of 9 per cent., and Scotland and Ireland excesses of 15,
and 11 per cent. respectively. The rainfall over Scotland was noteworthy
130 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
in that since 1906-07 there were only four wetter summers, viz., those
of 1922-23, 1923-24, 1926-27 and 1933-34 with 132, 134, 139 and 123
per cent, respectively. The map showing the distribution of the
rainfall as a percentage of the average is noteworthy for the area
with less than 70 per cent, stretching from the Wash to the Isle
of Wight and the areas with more than 130 per cent, between Dumfries
and Argyll and near Limerick. There was less than the average
over the greater part of England and Wales, but only in small areas in
Scotland and Ireland. Many stations in the south-east of England
recorded less than 80 per cent., the smallest percentages being 49
at Sherborne St. John, Hampshire, 56 at Ventnor, 62 at Camden
Square (London) and 63 at Compton, Sussex. At Torquay, where
heavy rains occurred on August 3rd to 4th, the total exceeded 120 per
cent. In Scotland the largest percentage values were 147 at Inveraray
Castle, 146 at Glasgow and 145 Eskdalemuir Observatory, in
Dumfries. In Ireland the variation was from 92 per cent, at Dublin
to 142 per cent, at Limerick.
For the seasonal year the percentage values were 86 for England
and Wales, 98 for Scotland, 92 for Ireland and 91 for the British
Isles as a whole. In each case the percentages were less than in any
year back to 1933-34. Over the British Isles as a whole there were
four drier seasonal vears since 1906-07, viz., those of 1908-09,1920-21,
1928-29 and 1933-34 with 89, 83, 90, and 87 per cent, respectively.
Over England and Wales there were only three drier seasonal vears
in a similar period, viz., 1920-21, 1928-29 and 1933-34 with 72, 83 and
79 per cent, respectively.
The maps of the rainfall during the seasonal year shows that most
stations recorded less than the average. The only area in England
and Wales with more than the average extended from the mouth
of the Humber to Newcastle-upon-Tyne. In Ireland there were small
areas with more than the average in the west of Galway and from
Limerick to Abbey Leix. In Scotland excesses were more widespread
especially in the west and north. Falls of more than 120 per cent,
were confined to Renfrewshire and Argyllshire, the largest values
being 133 at Inveraray Castle and 122 at Glasgow. On the other hand
falls of less than 80 per cent, occurred in Co. Mayo, Lancashire, Westmorland, near the Wash and over large areas around the mouth of
the Severn and from Cambridge to the Isle of Wight. The smallest
percentage values were 60 at Sherborne St. John, Hampshire, 73 at
Chewton Mendip, Somersetshire, and 74 at both Camden Square
(London) and Compton (Sussex).
It is of interest to note that while the seasonal
year 1937-38 included a dry winter, in the previous year it was the
summer which was dry. The general rainfall over the British Isles
for the twelve months April 1937 to March 1938 amounted to 36-4
SEASONAL RAINFALL. 131
inches or 88 per cent, of the average. This sequence of a dry winter
following a dry summer last occurred in 1933 and 1934, but the values
were then much smaller.
In British Rainfall, 1926, p. 125, values are given for the general
rainfall over England and Wales, Scotland and Ireland separately
back to 1906-7. In British Rainfall, 1936, pp. 131-2, similar values
are given for the British Isles from 1870 to 1936. The changes in
the seasonal distribution of rainfall become more apparent by taking
the means of these values for the British Isles for successive groups
of 10 years. From 1870 the excesses were contributed more especially
by wet summers ; from 1884 to 1892 dry winters predominated ;
from 1893 to 1910 the rainfall of both the summers and winters
approximated closely to the average ; from 1907 to 1919 the winters
were mainly wet with moderately dry summers, but more recently
the excesses were contributed mainly by wet summers, although the
rainfall of the winters have also slightly exceeded the average.

RELATION OF RAINFALL TO THE AVERAGE. 135
10. THE TOTAL FALL OF RAIN IN 1938 AND ITS
RELATION TO THE AVERAGE.
The total rainfall for the year is given for over 5,500 stations in
the British Isles in the General Table, Part III of this volume. As
many of these totals as practicable have been plotted on a new base
map on the scale of 16 miles, instead of 19 miles, to the inch. In
the districts of relatively few rainfall stations, each record has been
plotted, while in the more thickly represented areas some have had
to be omitted. Isohyetal lines, or lines of equal rainfall amount,
have been drawn giving a cartographical presentation of the distribution of the rainfall of the year over the land. The map is reproduced
on a smaller scale as the folding plate opposite this page. Such
a map is dominated by the orography of the country to such an extent
that not only is it complicated in itself, but it also closely resembles
its predecessors, the differences being noticeable only after detailed
examination and comparison. The characteristics of a particular
year are brought out better by a map such as that opposite p. 138,
in which the percentage ratio of the rainfall for the year to the local
average is shown. It has been coloured so that blue areas represent
regions where the fall in 1938 was more than the average, and red
areas represent regions where the fall in 1938 was less than the average
for the standard period 1881-1915. The statistics on which the latter
map is based are set out in Tables XXI and XXIV.
The percentage map for 1938 shows by means of continuous
lines the places where the values of the rainfall were, respectively,
80, 90, 100, 110, 120, 130, 140, 150, 160, 170 and 180 per cent, of the
average values. As much as 180 per cent, has not been noted in
any year since 1872, when 180 per cent, occurred at Osmotherly,
near Northallerton.
The rainfall over England was less than the average at most
stations to the south of a line roughly from Liverpool to the Wash
and along the east coast. In Devon and Cornwall, however, the rainfall exceeded the average and there was more than 110 per cent, in
the area between Okehampton and Torquay, where heavy rain
occurred on August 3rd and 4th. Less than 80 per cent, was recorded
in a number of areas of which the largest occurred to the west of
London, in the extreme east of Norfolk and in the valley of the River
Stour in Dorsetshire. There was more than 120 per cent, over three
large areas in the north-west as well as over part of Dartmoor.
Totals in, the Lake District and at Huddersfield exceeded 130 percent.
136 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
of the average. The extreme percentage values for stations in Tables
XXI and XXIV were 138 at Keswick and Grasmere in the Lake
District and 77 at Wellington College in Berkshire.
Over Wales the rainfall exceeded the average in most districts
and reached 130 per cent, in parts of Montgomeryshire. The range
was from 134 per cent, at Lake Vyrnwy to 92 per cent, at Abergavenny,
Monmouthshire.
The rainfall exceeded the average at most stations in Scotland,
but deficiencies occurred in the east, in parts of Morayshire, Banffshire
and Aberdeenshire. There was a large area in the west, equal in
extent to about half of Scotland, with more than 130 per cent. There
was a large area with over 150 per cent, extending from Fort Augustus
to Loch Katrine and two small areas, near Glasgow, and to the north
of Dumfries. The largest percentage values were 186 at Inveraray
Castle and 157 at both Glasgow and Loch Quoich (Glenquoich).
The value of 186 is the largest on record for any station in the British
Isles. The smallest percentage value was 87 at New Deer,
Aberdeenshire.
In Ireland the rainfall exceeded the average everywhere, except
near Dublin and Cork. There was more than 120 per cent, over a
large area mainly in the west and north, and more than 130 per cent,
near Londonderry, in Connemara and from Nenagh to Limerick.
The range was from 144 per cent, at Delphi Lodge in Connemara
to 98 per cent, at Roches Point, near Cork.
The large percentage values in the west of Scotland were due
more especially to the heavy and persistent rains of January, March,
May, June, July September and November. The total for March,
at Glenquoich (Loan) in Inverness-shire was as much as 50 • 03 inches
(see Section 8).
At the following stations, representative of a large area in the
west of Scotland, 1938 ranks as the wettest year since before 1868 :—

Use is made of the percentage values at the 251 stations of both
Tables XXI and XXIV to derive general values of the total rainfall
for the year over the British Isles and over the larger divisions
separately. These values are set out in the following table :—

For the British Isles as a whole, the rainfall of 1938 was 9 per
cent, in excess of the average, compared with an excess of 4 and 5
per cent, in 1937 and 1936. Whereas in 1937 and 1936 the rainfall
over Scotland was below the average, in 1938 there was an excess of
as much as 23 per cent. Since 1870 only four years have been as
wet in Scotland, viz., 1872, 1877, 1903 and 1928, with 134, 131, 129
and 123 per cent, respectively. The general rainfall over England
and Wales during 1938 closely approximated to the average, but was
less than that since 1934. Over Ireland and the British Isles as a
whole 1938 was the wettest year since 1930.
The general distribution of the actual rainfall, as shown on the
folding plate, is similar to that for other years, but the variation was
more marked than usual. In 1938 the range was from 15 inches,
in the Thames Estuary to over 210 inches in the Western Highlands
of Scotland.
Among the largest totals reported for the year and set out in
the General Table, Part III, the following may be mentioned :—

140 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
11.—EVAPORATION AND PERCOLATION IN 1938.
The evaporation gauges in use are square tanks sunk in the ground,
and the evaporation which is measured is that from a free water
surface. The standard gauge at Camden Square is 6 ft. square and
2 ft. deep, and is sunk so that the lip projects about 3 inches. A
photograph showing the Meteorological station, including the tank, at
Camden Square, was reproduced as the frontispiece of British Rainfall,
1921. The routine consists of the daily measurement of the level of the
water below a mark fixed in the tank. From the difference between
consecutive daily measurements, is deducted the rainfall for the same
period, as measured in an adjacent rain-gauge. The net depression
of the water-level in the tank during the interval of 24 hours is taken
to be a measure of the evaporation. Sometimes in winter the
" evaporation " comes out as a small negative quantity ; this is
regarded as due to the condensation of water from the superincumbent
air, and the corresponding measurements are marked " c" in the
tables. So far as is practicable, the level of the water is kept about
2 to 3 inches below the top of the lip. This necessitates water being
added in summer and syphoned off in winter. During the winter
months there is some difficulty in getting a reading when the water
freezes. In such cases the ice is broken away from the side of the
tank and the measurement of the water-level is made while the ice
is floating. If this cannot be done any morning, owing to the thickness
of the ice, a measurement is made as soon as possible (on a subsequent
morning), and the amount of evaporation calculated over the whole
period. The evaporation is small in winter, so that little information
is lost by adopting this procedure.
The accuracy of the measurements of evaporation depends on
the depth of water caught as rain in the evaporation tank being
identical with that measured directly in the rain-gauge alongside.
Some of the factors which are likely to vitiate the measurements of
evaporation were discussed in British Rainfall, 1925, pp. 127-128 and
in British Rainfall, 1931, pp. 268-274.
EVAPORATION. 141
The list of stations given in Table XXV, for which details of
the evaporation are available for 1938, is the same as last year.
The monthly and annual means for the fourteen stations are set
out in the last column of the table. For the year the mean evaporation
is 17-0 inches. The evaporation at individual stations during 1938 was
more than the average except at Talla Water and Kennick, where
the evaporation for the year was 1 -9 inch and '9 inch less than the
average 1908-1938. Over the British Isles generally the sunshine was
above the average in March, April, October and December and slightly
below the average for the year as a whole. At eleven stations the
greatest amount of evaporation occurred in June, and at Petersfield,
Ormesby St. Michael and Amlaird Filters in August. The four
months May to August accounted for 62 per cent, of the total evaporation for the year.
Evaporation at Camden Square (London). The monthly evaporation values for the 54 years, 1885 to 1938, are set out in Table XXVII.
The record is the longest available in this country. For 1938 the
evaporation of 15-87 inches was -37 inch more than the average for
the 35 years (1885-1919). The total evaporation was largest in June
with 3-44 ins. May, July and August also had total of more than
2 inches. In March, April, June, August and October the totals
exceeded the average. The differences from the monthly averages
varied from + -53 inch in June to — -40 inch in July. The total for
March of 1-18 inch was the largest ever recorded in that month. This
reflects the exceptional warmth of that month.
Percolation gaiiges. In Table XXVI the amount of water which
percolates through a definite depth of soil is given. The list of stations
is the same as for 1937. Details of the percolation gauges and th<
type of soil are set out in the footnotes, in British Rainfall, 1924, p. 128,
in British Rainfall, 1932, p. 143, in British Rainfall, 1934, p. 137 and
in British Rainfall, 1937, p. 133. The difference between percolation
and rainfall is largely to be accounted for by evaporation, but evaporation as determined in this way (known as " calculated evaporation ")
is not strictly comparable with that given in the earlier table. There
is no free water surface present in the percolation gauge, but during
dry periods the surface soil is nearly dry, so that practically no evaporation can take place. In dry years the calculated evaporation, as
obtained from the percolation gauges, will be less than similar values
for wet years, and less than the values obtained from the water surface
in a tank. In 1938 the mean value from the 15 percolation gauges was
14-98 inches, compared with 15-34 inches in 1937.
At Fleam Dyke, the two percolation gauges are each 36 inches
deep composed of 9 inches of soil over well-rammed chalk, and both
have surface areas of one-thousandth of an acre as at Rothamsted.
The surface of one gauge is covered with short turf and the other with
14- BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
soil which is kept free from all vegetation. The treatment was the
reverse of that in 1937. The difference between the percolation
through the two gauges was largest during the three months September
to November, when the turfed gauge gave no percolation and the
bare gauge as much as 4 -08 inches. The mean percolation for the two
gauges amounted to 42 per cent, of the rainfall during the months
January to March and October to December, but was only 10 per cent,
during the summer six months. There was no percolation through
either gauge during the five months April to August.
At Compton the total percolation through the gauge (which
consists of 10 inches of soil, on which grass is grown, over 2 feet of
well-rammed chalk) was nil for the period March 15th to October 16th.
Percolation for the three months February, November and December
exceeded the rainfall during those months. At Farlington there was
only a small amount of percolation during the eight months March to
October. At Rothamsted there was no measurable percolation during
the five months March to July. At Harrogate, where more rain occurred
there was some percolation in each month, although the total March
to May only amounted to -39 inch. At Craibstone the manured
and limed gauge gave only -07 inch of percolation during the four
months March to June.
The records from the Farlington gauges continue to show a
consistent diminution in the amount of percolation as the depth of the
gauge increases. This suggests that in order to form a reliable estimation of the amount of the rain which actually reaches the underground
water table, a gauge not less than 6 feet deep should be employed.
On the other hand the 20 inch and 60 inch gauges at Rothamsted
continue to record approximately the same percolation, while that
through the 40 inch gauge is somewhat greater. The differences are
accounted for by variations in the composition and structure of the
soil of the gauges, and in this case it seems reasonable to conclude that
the 20-inch gauge is of sufficient depth to give a good indication of
the water likely to penetrate the sub-soil there.
The three gauges at Craibstone, each 40 inches deep, were under
pasture in 1938 as in 1932. The amount of percolation was least
where the soil was unmanured.

AIR MINISTRY, METEOROLOGICAL OFFICE
GENERAL TABLE OP TOTAL RAINFALL IN 1938. H9
EXPLANATORY NOTE.
The Divisions of Great Britain in the General Table are the same
as those adopted by the Registrars-General of England and Scotland.
They are shown on the map facing this page.
The counties are divided up into numbered areas, corresponding
to the river-basins. An account of these river-divisions for England
and Wales was given in a special article in Part I of British Rainfall,
1910 (pp. 37-66), and a similar article on the river-divisions of Scotland
and Ireland appeared in Part I of British Rainfall, 1911 (pp. 40-72),
with detailed maps showing all the river-divisions in each Division.
Lists were also included showing the various river-divisions in each of
the main catchment areas over the British Isles. The areas of the river
basins of the Great Ouse and tributaries in Norfolk and Cambridge were
redetermined in 1928 ; the values printed in this volume and in the
volume for 1927 differ slightly from those published in British Rainfall
from 1910 to 1926. The sequence of river divisions in Great Britain
proceeds anti-clockwise from the Solway Firth. For example, in the
West Riding of Yorkshire we have four divisions for rivers flowing to
the Irish Sea, followed by eight divisions for rivers tributary to the
Humber.
The stations in each river-division are arranged nearly in the order
of their latitude from South to North. For convenience, the first
name defining a station is that of a place which can be found on
any large scale map ; occasionally it is in an adjoining county. The
second name is added to fix the site of observation more closely,
e.g., " Haslemere (Clammer Hill) " means Clammer Hill, near
Haslemere.
Stations the names of which are printed in small capitals, e.g.,
KENSINGTON PALACE, are clirnatological stations in association with
the Meteorological Office.* For the majority of these a summary of
the clirnatological observations is printed in the Monthly Weather
Report.
In some cases the records have been received from a local rainfall
organization. This has been indicated by a footnote.
In the column headed " Diameter," figures in old style type
indicate the length of the sides of rectangular gauges.
The letter D signifies that daily observations have been received
and are complete for the year ; d, that less complete daily records
have been received ; w indicates that weekly readings have been
received, and M that the gauge is read only once a month, usually
on the 1st.
(R) denotes that the gauge is a mechanical one the correct reading
of which depends on the mechanism being in working order.______
* In the case of Division I small capitals are also used to indicate the names of
London Boroughs.
150 BRITISH RA1M ALL, 1938.
The use of mechanical gauges for measuring the total rainfall is
not recommended by the Meteorological Office and the number of
records marked (R) is therefore small.
(+A) denotes that the amount of rainfall is taken from an ordinary
gauge but that the equipment also includes an autographic
instrument.
Gauges which have been inspected and found to be over-exposed
on moorland have been so marked. It is likely that there are
other gauges (notably some of those at lighthouses) which are also
over-exposed.
The column for the total rainfall in millimetres, which was a
feature of the corresponding table in the volumes for the years 1914
to 1925, has been omitted since 1926, and the space thus set free is
devoted to a column for average annual totals, expressed in inches,
to the nearest tenth of an inch. The averages are printed in ordinary
type if they are either the mean of the annual values for the standard
period 1881 to 1915, or, in the case of shorter records, if they have been
reduced by the accepted method to the standard period. In such
cases all the available information has been used in the computation
of the averages, means for stations with short records being weighted
to the standard period 1881 to 1915 by comparison with nearby
stations with records complete from 1881 to date. If the computation
has been made outside the office the average is printed in italics,
since it may subsequent!}' be revised slightly. The average is also
given in italics if it is the mean for twenty or more years other than
the standard period or if the mean has been weighted to some other
period. Averages for the standard period of 35 years 1881 to 1915,
are always preferred for entry in the table. Many of the averages
printed in italics have been kindly computed by Mr. H. P. Cornish
of Keswick and these are weighted to the standard period 1881 to
1915.
A few entries of total rain will be found in brackets—indicating
that the values have been corrected, e.g., when appreciable snow has
been lost, when the amount for a short period has been computed
from other records, or when the gauge has been moved a short distance
during the year. A record is not as a rule printed if the gauge has been
moved more than a mile, or if there is a greater difference than 50 ft.
of altitude between the two positions.
A note of interrogation (?) implies doubt, not necessarily error.
The absence of information is indicated bv ..

BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938
PART IV.
278 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
RAINFALL OVER THE BRITISH ISLES DURING THE DRY
YEARS 1854 to 1858.
By JOHN GLASSPOOLE, M.Sc., Ph.D.
An examination of the annual general values for England
(Glasspoole, 1928) shows that while there have been many instances
of two consecutive dry years, longer runs have been infrequent.
Since 1816 there have been only three occasions of more than two
consecutive dry years : the three years 1849 to 1851, the five years
1854 to 1858, and the four years 1887 to 1890. It is also noteworthy
that while 1921 stands out as the driest year since 1816, the period
1854 to 1858 gives the driest two, three, four and five consecutive
years.
Maps showing the distribution of rainfall over the British Isles
are available back to 1868 (Glasspoole, 1926a) and general values are
also available only since then (Glasspoole, 1923). The rainfall of 1921
(Glasspoole, 1922) and of the three driest consecutive years since 1868
(Glasspoole, 1924, a) have been studied in detail, but longer dry spells
have not previously been examined. So that while the rainfall of
both the dry periods 1921 and 1887 to 1890 has been defined with
precision, there is no comparable data about the long dry period
1854 to 1858. In fact, it has previously been assumed that there
was little prospect of preparing annual maps for these early years.
A knowledge of the rainfall experienced in various localities during
this run of dry years is of practical value as an indication of what
may recur. It is of importance to those concerned with the maintenance of adequate water supplies, or power from hydro-electric
schemes and from general agricultural aspects.
It was this dry weather during the period 1854 to 1858 that
impressed on the late G. J. Symons the importance of collecting rainfall
records and finally resulted in the formation of the British Rainfall
Organization about 1860. Symons left numerous records covering
these dry years, but curiously no published summaries of the data.
Preparation of Annual Percentage Values.
In evaluating the rainfall at individual stations for the years
1854 to 1858 as a percentage of the average* the problem has been
to evaluate an average appropriate to these early records. Only a
few records have been maintained continuously from 1854 to date
and some of these, owing to changes in the exposure of the rain-gauge,
are not homogeneous. As a result of an earlier investigation (Glasspoole, 1924, b) the homogeneous records extending back to 1868
were already known and their appropriate averages for the period
1881 to 1915 available. These records, where they covered the years
* The average used throughout the paper is that for the standard period 1881 to 1915.
RAINFALL OVER THE BRITISH ISLES DURING THE DRY YEARS 1854 TO 1858. 279
1854 to 1858, were used with the existing averages.
The next problem was to find averages appropriate for shorter
records covering the years 1854 to 1858. This was done in three
further stages :—
(b) Where the record covered, the decade 1860 to 1869. In such
cases the average was computed by comparing the mean annual
amount for this decade with the percentage of the period 1881 to 1915
shown on the decadal maps (Glasspoole, 1933).
(c) Where the record covered the decade 1850 to 1859, but not that for
1860 to 1869, the decadal map for 1850 to 1859 was used.
(d) Where the record was even shorter and did not cover the decade
1850 to 1859, the appropriate average was read off the large scale
survey maps of average annual rainfall.
From this information the corresponding annual percentage values
were computed and plotted on maps on a scale of 60 miles to 1 inch.
An examination of these values on the maps brought to light a few
inconsistencies and in these cases new averages were computed,
limiting attention to shorter periods as described under (b), (c) or (d)
in that sequence. The number of stations for which percentages
were computed under the headings (a), (b), (c) and (d) were 11, 49, 47
and 15 respectively, a total of 122. In addition percentage values
were used for a number of stations where records were available for
most but not all of the years 1854 to 1858.
It was found that there was sufficient information to show the
percentage distribution of the rainfall in all parts of the British Isles,
except the northern half of Scotland and northern and central Wales.
Such values as were available in these areas were consistent and
fitted in with the general type of distribution which occurred in
other parts of the country with ample data. A study of the type of
distribution prevailing on each annual map enabled the lines to be
drawn over the areas of few records with a fair degree of confidence.
Thus in the case of the map for 1854, while the records in Wales merely
indicate that most of that area received over 80 per cent, it is possible
to deduce that as much as 100 per cent, occurred in part of North
Wales because the other mountainous districts, e.g. the Lake District
and the north-west of the British Isles, generally gave relatively
large percentage values. A similar argument enables the 70 per cent,
line to be drawn in that area in the case of 1855. This line of argument
is supported by an earlier statistical investigation on the " Relation
between annual rainfall over Europe and that at Oxford and at
Glenquoich (Western Highlands of Scotland) ", (Glasspooie, 1926, b).
Annual rainfall maps 1854 to 1858.
Maps showing the rainfall of the individual years 1854 to 1858,
as a percentage of the average, are reproduced as Figs. 1 to 5.
280 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
RainfaU, 1854 (Fig. 1).—The main feature of the distribution is
the large area with less than 80 per cent, over much of England
extending from the south coast across central England to the east

of the Pennines. There is a large area in the south of England with

excess of rainfall in the north-west of the country. The type of
rainfall distribution has many points of resemblance to that of 1921,
when the Azores high pressure system was generally at a more northerly
latitude and depressions passed further to the north of the British Isles
than usual.

then not so well marked as in the two previous years.

Rainfall, 1858 (Fig. 5).—For 1858 there is a deficiency of rainfall
in nearly all parts of the British Isles, although the deficiency is not
quite so widespread as in 1855. The type of distribution in 1858
resembles that of 1854, the largest areas with less than 80 per cent.

also less than 80 per cent, over much of the Pennines, over a large
area across Inverness-shire to Aberdeen and near Galway. The
smallest percentage values are :—60 at Hastings, 66 at Selborne, in
Hampshire, 67 at Buxted Park, Sussex, and 68 at Sittingbourne,
in Kent.
Annual General Rainfall, 1854 to 1858.
The general rainfall has been evaluated by taking the mean of
values read off the map for 240 representative points over the British
Isles. These mean values and the corresponding values for the dry
years 1921 and 1887 to 1890 are given in Table I.

1921 was the driest year over England and England and Wales ;
1855 over Scotland and Ireland; while 1887 was slightly drier than
1855 over the British Isles as a whole.
The driest two consecutive years were 1854 and 1855 over England
and Wales, and 1855 and 1856 over Scotland and Ireland, in all cases
the mean annual value being about 80 per cent. The corresponding
values for 1887 and 1888 were about 86 per cent. Moreover the mean
rainfall for the five years 1854 to 1858 is comparable with that for
286 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938.
the two years 1887 to 1888. This illustrates that the five years
1854 to 1858 gave a deficiency as remarkable as that of 1887 to 1888,
but continued for five years instead of two only.
It is of interest to compare the estimates for England given in

In the earlier investigation the emphasis was placed on the
general trend of the fluctuations from decade to decade and century
to century. It is satisfactory to note, therefore, that over the whole
period the totals are identical. The more detailed estimates of
Table I should be adopted in place of the earlier values.
Monthly rainfall over England and Wales, 1854 to 1858.
Utilising the values given in Table I for England and Wales, it is
possible to give estimates of the annual general rainfall in inches.
Monthly percentage values for this period have already been published
(Nicholas and Glasspoole, 1932). From this information monthly
values in inches have been computed to indicate the incidence of
rainfall during this dry period. The values are given in Table III,
together with the mean monthly values. Table III indicates that
the small annual totals were due mainly to the winter and spring
months November, December, January, February and March. Over
the whole period the rainfall of the seven months April to October
slightly exceeded the average. This incidence of the rainfall confirms
the deficiency of west and south-west winds, already commented
upon, since these winds are on the average strongest and most frequent
in the winter rather than the summer half year.
It is also worthy of note that the driest months in Table III are
January, 1855, and the Aprils of 1854 and 1855 with 0-6, 0-7 and
0-6 inch respectively. There are, however, appreciably drier individual months. Thus the general rainfall over England and Wales
was 0-1 inch in both February, 1891, and June, 1925; O3 inch in
April, 1893, April, 1912, March, 1929, and April, 1938, and 0-4 inch in
May, 1896, February, 1921, and June, 1921.

Mean annual rainfall, 1854 to 1858.
The distribution of the mean annual rainfall experienced during
the five years 1854 to 1858 is shown in Fig 6. In the construction
of this map the actual values have been used and in addition
values have been obtained for points on the maps for which no records
are available, by reading percentages off the annual maps, Figs. 1-5.
The map indicates that the total rainfall for this period reached
the average in only small areas, viz., at Holkham, Norfolk ; at Macclesfield ; near Kirkcudbright; in the Outer Hebrides and in part of
Skye. There was less than 90 per cent, over practically the whole of
Ireland and of Wales ; over about half of Scotland, mainly the northwest ; and about two-thirds of England, mainly the southern half
but also in the north-west. The mean was less than 80 per cent,
along the south-east coast of England, in south Wales, the English
Lake District, near Greenock, in part of Caithness, over a large area
around Galway and near Dublin.
The smallest percentage values are 74 at Galway, and 77 at
Nosshead, Caithness, at Killaloe, Co. Clare, and at Greenock.
288 BRITISH RAINFALL, 1938
The abnormality of the rainfall during the period 1854 to 1858
can be illustrated by comparing the annual mean shown in Fig. 6 with
the annual values for the following eighty years. The distribution
shown in Fig. 6 would be remarkable if it applied to one year only.
In the series 1859 to 1938 there are only seven years, viz., 1864, 1870,
1887, 1893, 1902, 1921 and 1933, with either comparable or smaller
values over the country generally. The mean of the five years 1854
to 1858 was less than that of each individual year in the period 1859
to 1938 with but seven exceptions, and these were all separated by
wet years.

MAGAZINE
*~phe monthly magazine for all who are interested
in the weather.
THE METEOROLOGICAL MAGAZINE was founded in
1866 by the late George James Symons, F.R.S., founder of the
British Rainfall Organization, under the title of Symons' s
Monthly Meteorological Magazine.
With the issue for February, 1939, a new policy has been
adopted. Certain features which were deemed to be of little
general interest have been omitted, a "Notes and News"
column has been introduced, and in future authoritative nontechnical articles upon a variety of meteorological subjects
and problems will be regularly included. The cover and type
display has also been improved to increase the attractiveness
of the journal.
Recent articles include —
The Thunderstorms of August, 1938.
Sunshine Records at Health Resorts.
The Weather of 1938.
Climatology and Forecasting.
Climate Changes.
Atmospheric Electricity.
